The Six Tongues Protocol

by Issac Davis

Copyright 2026 Issac Daniel Davis. All rights reserved.


For my Mom, whose support made me the man I am.

For my Grandfathers, for their love, their strength,
and the names I carry.



Chapter 1: Protocol Handshake

The smell of stale coffee sat on Marcus Chen's tongue like a warning he refused to read. Three hours cold. The good part was gone—the bitter warmth, the roasted-bean kick that hit the back of the throat like a handshake from someone who meant it. What was left was flat and nothing. The mug sat at his elbow, a ring of brown residue marking the tidal line of his last sip, sometime around midnight. It was now 3:14 AM. Saturday. Or technically Sunday, if you were the kind of person who cared about things like calendars, sleep cycles, and the basic operating parameters of human biology.

Marcus was not that kind of person.

He'd left the apartment Thursday evening. The window had been open—the one in the kitchen that stuck unless you lifted and angled it just right, a two-handed operation that his landlord called "character" and Marcus called "a building code violation." A warm breeze had been coming through it, carrying the smell of eucalyptus and fog and the salt-damp sweetness of the Sunset District at dusk. He'd paused with his bag on his shoulder, one hand on the sill, and the breeze had touched his face the way breezes touch faces in moments that are about to become the last of something, and he hadn't known it, but his body had. His shoulders had dropped. His breathing had slowed. For three seconds he had stood there feeling warm air on his skin and being, without knowing the word for it, grateful.

Then he'd seen Bryce's photo on the wall by the door. Blue plaid shirt, chin on fist, easy grin. The one from the company retreat, before the diagnosis, before January, before the empty desk and the silence that replaced the loudest laugh in the office. Bryce Davidson, who had once told Marcus that the meaning of life was "good ribs and a friend who'll drive you to the hospital" and who had been right about both.

Marcus had looked at the photo. Looked at the apartment—the mattress on the floor, the expired yogurt, the Sriracha, the three monitors dark and waiting, the bass guitar leaning against the wall by the bathroom door, tuned to standard from a two-semester experiment in being someone who played music, never sold because some failures are more honest than the things you replace them with. The window with its warm breeze. His whole life in one room. Not much. But held together by the specific gravity of someone who had chosen it, piece by piece, because each piece had a reason even if the reasons were small.

He'd closed the window. Pulled the blinds. Left.

That was Thursday. This was Sunday. He had not gone back.

The terminal blinked its familiar green-on-black rhythm. Sixteen hours into a voluntary shift, and the only thing in his life that made complete sense was a blinking cursor on a black screen.

Something was wrong with the logs, annoyingly so.

Not dramatically wrong. Not the kind of wrong that set off alarms and paged the incident response team and got you a phone call from a VP at 2 AM. This was the other kind of wrong—the kind that sat in the back of your skull like a splinter, not quite painful enough to demand action but impossible to ignore. A feeling. An itch in the pattern.

Marcus leaned forward, scrolling through authentication records with the focused intensity of a man who had replaced most of his social life with packet analysis. The overhead fluorescents had been off for hours; the only light came from his three monitors, painting his face in alternating shades of green and black. Somewhere on the floor below, a security guard was doing rounds. Marcus could hear the squeak of his shoes on tile, metronomic and lonely.

There. Line 4,847. Gotchu.

A sequence of authorization tokens that didn't quite fit. Not forged—he'd have caught that immediately. Not corrupted—the checksums were clean. These tokens were *valid*. Properly signed, properly timestamped, properly routed through every authentication layer in the stack. They just shouldn't exist.

He pulled up the routing table. The packets were traversing an unauthorized channel—a pathway through the network that wasn't documented in any architecture diagram he'd ever seen. Not a backdoor, exactly. More like a hallway that had always been there, behind a wall nobody had thought to knock on.

Marcus frowned. He'd been a senior security engineer at Kershaw-Lin for four years, and before that, three years at a startup that got acqui-hired by Google. He'd seen elegant attacks and sloppy ones, insider threats and nation-state operations, zero-days and one-day wonders. He knew what malicious traffic looked like.

This wasn't malicious. That was what bothered him.

The pattern was too... deliberate. Too clean. Like someone had threaded a needle through seven layers of authentication without breaking a single one, not because they were trying to bypass security, but because they understood the architecture so deeply that they could walk between the rules. The way a jazz musician plays between the notes—not wrong, just outside.

Almost beautiful, actually. If it weren't deeply, fundamentally unauthorized.

He reached for the coffee, remembered it was dead-cold, shrugged, took a sip, and set it back down. His tongue still carried the taste of it—stale, mineral, faintly metallic. The taste of late nights and bad decisions, but how could he not? It is the taste of his actual life.

"Found you," he muttered, highlighting the sequence. His fingers moved across the keyboard, composing a forensic trace, preparing to follow the thread back to its origin. The unauthorized channel had a source, and that source had a signature, and that signature would tell him—

The screen went white.

Not the blue-screen-of-death white. He'd seen that a thousand times—the resigned crash of an operating system admitting defeat after hours of running the CPU to the limit. Not the monitor-failure white, either, which came with a pop and a smell of burnt plastic. This was different. This was *everywhere* white. The screen, yes, but also the walls. The ceiling. The back of his hands when he lifted them from the keyboard. The floor beneath his rolling chair. The air itself, as if someone had deleted the color channel from reality and left only the brightness slider cranked to maximum.

Marcus stood. Or tried to. The chair was gone. The desk was gone. The floor was a concept rather than a surface—he could feel pressure beneath his feet, but when he looked down, there was only light.

Then, in the light's place, there was nothing. An abstract yet visceral silence.

Not silence—silence is the absence of sound, and this wasn't absence. This was convergence. Every ambient noise—the hum of the servers in the next room, the distant whine of the building's HVAC, the security guard's squeaking shoes, Marcus's own breathing—all of it folded inward, compressing into a single sustained tone. A frequency he felt in his molars, in the bones behind his eyes, in the base of his spine. It vibrated there, patient and precise, like a tuning fork struck against the edge of existence.

*What do you intend?*

The question arrived without words. It wasn't heard. It wasn't thought. It was experienced the way you experience gravity or thirst—a fundamental condition of the moment, unavoidable and absolute.

Marcus tried to answer. Tried to form a sentence—*I intend to go home, I intend to find out what's happening, I intend to not die in whatever this is*—but the words dissolved before they could organize. The question wasn't asking for language. It was asking for something underneath language, something Marcus didn't have a name for.

And then — impossibly, absurdly, in the middle of the dissolution of everything he'd ever known — he felt a warm breeze.

Not from outside. Not from the open window he'd closed on Thursday. From *inside*. From the place in his chest where Thursday still lived — the eucalyptus air, Bryce's grin on the wall, the three seconds of standing still and being grateful for a life that fit in one room. The warmth rose through him like a seed cracking open, and for one fraction of a second that lasted longer than it should have, the question stopped asking and started *listening*, as if the honesty of that feeling — small, specific, unglamorous, real — was the answer it had been looking for.

He fell.

Not through a hole. Not off an edge. Through the *floor of what was real*, as if reality were a membrane and he'd been standing on its surface tension his whole life without knowing it, and now the tension had broken, and the fall was neither down nor up but *through*—through layers of something that flickered past too fast to process. Frequencies woven into geometries woven into meanings.

For one disorienting mind-bending moment, Marcus saw himself from the outside: a data point, a single blinking cursor in an enormous system, being routed from one process to another. He was a packet. He was being *transmitted*.

Then the tone stopped, and Marcus Chen hit stone.

* * *

Cold. Hard. The specific cold-and-hard of natural stone that hasn't seen sunlight in a very long time. Marcus's cheek pressed against it, and the sensation was so concrete, so aggressively physical after the dissolving abstraction of whatever had just happened, that he almost wept with relief.

He was somewhere real. Or at least somewhere solid.

He lay still for three breaths, running the kind of rapid assessment that crisis training beats into you: can I move my fingers? Yes. Toes? Yes. Any pain beyond the general everything-hurts of being flung through the membrane of reality? No. Okay. Good.

The smell hit him next, musty paper with a hint of time and animal hide.

Old books. Not the polite, nostalgic old-bookstore smell of aging paper and dust. This was deeper—leather and vellum and something organic, like the books themselves were alive and breathing. Layered over it: ozone, sharp and electric, the way the air smells before a lightning strike. And beneath both, something he had no name for. A third scent that wasn't quite a scent, more like a pressure in his sinuses, a resonance. As if the room itself were humming at a frequency just below human hearing, and his nose was picking up the vibration.

*Magic?*

No. Focus. Assess.

Marcus opened his eyes and pushed himself up onto his palms. Stone floor, smooth but uneven, the kind of surface shaped by centuries of footsteps rather than machines. Curved ceiling above, not built but *grown*—crystal formations that caught light from no obvious source and refracted it into soft, directionless illumination. The walls were the same crystal, but carved. Shelves upon shelves, stretching upward into a ceiling that might have been thirty feet high or might have been infinite, each shelf holding leather-bound volumes that—

—*hummed*.

Not loudly. Not obviously. But Marcus had spent his career listening for signals in noise, and the books were putting out a signal. A low, complex, polyphonic vibration that felt like standing inside a chord. Each book on a slightly different frequency. Thousands of them, harmonizing into something too vast to hear as music, but that his body recognized the way it recognized heat or pressure.

He got to his knees. His hands were shaking.

"You're finally awake."

The voice came from above and behind him, and it carried the specific exhausted annoyance of someone who'd been waiting longer than they thought they'd have to. Marcus turned.

A raven sat on the nearest shelf, about four feet above his head.

Not a normal raven—Marcus had seen ravens in the Presidio, big glossy birds that moved with the arrogant intelligence of animals who knew they were smarter than most people gave them credit for. This one was *larger*, easily twice the size, with feathers so dark they seemed to drink the crystal light rather than reflect it, shifting between black and deep violet as she moved. Her eyes were polished obsidian—not metaphorically; the irises actually looked like mineral, smooth and reflective and too bright, studying him with an intelligence that was sharp enough to cut.

And she was wearing—

Marcus blinked.

A graduation cap. Miniature, perfectly proportioned, sitting at a jaunty angle between her ears. A monocle glinted over one eye, held by some mechanism he couldn't identify—possibly magic, possibly sheer stubbornness. And around her throat, a bowtie. Black silk, neatly knotted.

A raven in academic regalia. Looking at him like he was a student who'd shown up twenty minutes late to her seminar.

"Took you long enough," she said, in a language Marcus did not know. Not one word of it matched anything in his experience—not the phonemes, not the cadence, not the tonal structure. And yet he understood every syllable perfectly, as if the meaning had bypassed his ears entirely and landed directly in his comprehension. Her beak hadn't moved the way a bird's beak should when producing human speech. The words simply *arrived*, the same way the Protocol's question had arrived—not through air, but through something underneath air.

"I'm Polly, Fifth Circle Keeper of the Archives." She hopped along the shelf edge, talons clicking on crystal, the monocle catching light with each tilt of her head. "You're Marcus Chen, systems engineer, age thirty-two, last known location: San Francisco, Earth. Correct?"

Marcus sat up slowly. His threat assessment was running on autopilot—*armed? talons, beak, unknown capability. exits? one doorway, behind her shelf. hostiles? one entity, avian, sentient, dressed better than me. environment? unknown, enclosed, crystalline, possibly underground*—even as the rest of his brain tried to process the fact that a raven in a graduation cap had just recited his personnel file in a language that shouldn't exist.

"How do you—"

"The Protocol told us you were coming." She cocked her head—a sharp corvid motion, one obsidian eye fixed on him with the unblinking focus of a creature that could count, categorize, and judge in a single glance. "It always does. You're not the first, won't be the last." A pause. Her feathers ruffled, then settled—the smallest possible gesture of something softer than irritation. "Welcome to Aethermoor."

Marcus's mind did what it always did when confronted with the impossible: it reached for the simplest explanation.

"This is a hallucination," he said flatly. "I've been awake for twenty-two hours. That coffee was three hours past drinkable. I'm having a psychotic break and I'm probably drooling on my keyboard right now while the security guard calls an ambulance."

Polly tilted her head to the other side. "Occam's Razor would suggest you've been isekai'd to a fantasy world where magic is actually cryptographic protocol architecture, but sure, go with 'psychotic break' if it helps you process." She spread her wings—wider than he expected, each one longer than his arm—and dropped from the shelf to the floor in front of him with a grace that was half flight, half controlled fall. On the ground, standing at her full height, she came up to about his knee. The graduation cap didn't fall off. The monocle stayed put. "Come on. If you're going to survive here, you need to learn the Six Sacred Tongues. And I don't have centuries to wait."

"Survive?" Marcus didn't move yet. "That implies I could *not* survive. What kills me here? The magic? The architecture?"

Something flickered across Polly's feathers—a tightening, a compression, plumage pressing flat. Fast enough that a less observant person would have missed it, but Marcus was a security engineer. He read micro-expressions the way he read log files—habitually, involuntarily, always.

It was fear.

Not fear of him. Fear *for* him.

"In Aethermoor," Polly said carefully, "your existence is verified by the Protocol every zero-point-three seconds. It's a heartbeat check. Continuous authentication against your baseline state. Pass, and you exist. Fail..." She let the word hang. "You flicker. Fail hard enough, and you cease. Not die. *Cease*. Like a process that gets killed—no graceful shutdown, no error log, no memory. Just... gone."

Marcus stared at her.

"And right now," she continued, "you're running on a guest pass. Temporary authentication. The Protocol is giving you about seventy-two hours to establish a permanent baseline, and after that—" She ruffled her feathers in a *poof* gesture. "—no more Marcus Chen."

"I need to stand up," Marcus said. Not because standing would help. Because lying on a stone floor while a raven in a bowtie explained the terms of his continued existence felt like the wrong posture for the conversation.

He pushed himself to his feet. His legs were unsteady. Polly watched him from below with that unblinking corvid gaze—head tilted, monocle glinting—and then, without warning, *changed*.

It wasn't dramatic. No flash of light, no swirl of dark energy, no cinematic transformation sequence. One moment she was a raven standing at his knee. The next, she was *unfolding*—feathers flowing upward like ink in water, shape stretching and reorganizing with the casual efficiency of a window being resized on a desktop. Wings extended, body lengthened, and in the space between two heartbeats, a girl stood where the bird had been.

Couldn't be older than twenty, though something in her eyes suggested that was a wildly inaccurate assessment. The feathers remained—glossy and black, growing from her scalp and cascading down her shoulders in an iridescent curtain. Wings folded against her back, longer now, proportioned for this larger form. The graduation cap was gone. The monocle was gone. The bowtie was gone—absorbed into the transformation like accessories being de-rendered. But the eyes hadn't changed at all. Polished obsidian, smooth and reflective and too bright. Exactly as sharp as they'd been in the raven.

Her expression was still annoyed. But it was a layered annoyance, the kind that comes from caring about something enough to be frustrated by it. Her fingers were human-shaped, if slightly too long, the nails dark and faintly iridescent. She extended a hand.

"Easier this way," she said. "Hands are useful."

He took her hand.

It was warm. Solid. Real in a way that the white void and the falling and the stone floor hadn't been—real the way another person's touch is real, specific and present and alive. Her grip was stronger than he expected. She pulled him up like he weighed nothing.

"What are the Six Sacred Tongues?" he asked, because when the world stops making sense, you start with definitions. That was his nature. His training. His *survival instinct*, dressed up as curiosity.

"Domain-separated authorization channels." Polly watched his face as she said it, and when his eyebrows did the thing—the specific eyebrow-raise of an engineer who just heard a familiar concept in an impossible context—she grinned. Wide and sharp and delighted.

"What?" Marcus said. "You thought magic would be all wands and fireballs? Incantations and pointy hats?"

"*Caw!*" Polly spread her wings, the sound ringing off the crystal ceiling like a judge's gavel made of feathers. "Buddy," she said, the feathers catching that sourceless crystal light and throwing it back in prismatic fragments, "this is a serious universe. We have *infrastructure*."

She turned and walked toward the doorway, wings folding neatly against her back. Her footsteps were silent on the stone. Marcus followed, because there was nothing else to do, and because something in her voice—beneath the sarcasm, beneath the practiced irreverence of someone who'd given this orientation speech before—was urgent.

The corridor stretched ahead, walls of crystallized light, floors that looked transparent but held his weight, doorways that appeared only when approached and vanished when passed. Somewhere inside the crystal there was a soft ticking, as if the place were keeping time by consensus.

One section of wall was open — not a window, not a balcony, just a gap in the crystal where the corridor forgot to be enclosed. Marcus glanced through it and stopped walking.

Outside was impossible.

A sky lined with soft auroral light, violet and gold, pulsing in rhythm with something he could feel in his sternum. Below the corridor — far below, too far — a river of pale blue luminescence wound between structures that weren't quite buildings and weren't quite mountains. Some of them floated. Not dramatically, not with engines or magic circles or any visible mechanism. They just hung there, like someone had paused gravity mid-sentence and forgotten to finish the thought. Meadows grew on the undersides of several, grass pointing down toward nothing, as if the ground had its own opinions about which direction mattered.

*What the actual—*

A bridge of crystal arced between two of the floating landmasses, and something moved across it. Not a person. Not a vehicle. Something with too many legs and a quiet, purposeful gait, like a patrol making rounds through a world that had decided architecture was optional.

Marcus's mouth opened. Closed. Opened again.

*Rivers flowing sideways. Mountains that hover. Grass growing upside down. This isn't a building. This is a planet someone built inside a snow globe and forgot to include the laws of physics.*

"Don't stare," Polly said from ahead. "You'll have time for the existential landscape crisis later. Right now you're on a clock."

Marcus pulled his eyes away from the impossible geography and kept walking.

His legs moved on autopilot while his brain ran triage on the last five minutes of his life. Winged girl. Crystal library. Seventy-two hours to not *cease existing*. Fine. He'd had worse Saturdays. Not many. But some.

"So," he said to Polly's back, because silence made the impossibility louder, "do you have coffee here?"

Polly stopped mid-stride and turned, her head tilting at that corvid angle again. "Caw-fee?"

"Coffee. Hot bean water. The thing that keeps engineers from lying down on the floor and accepting death."

"Is it... a weapon?"

"Some people's coffee, yeah." Marcus rubbed the back of his neck. "I didn't get to finish mine. Before the—" He gestured vaguely at everything. "Before the white void-pit-thing and the falling through reality I had a mug on my desk. Wasn't even good coffee. I just..." He trailed off with a sigh suddenly aware that what he was actually saying was *I want something familiar because nothing here is*.

Polly studied him for a long moment. Those obsidian eyes were unreadable, but her feathers shifted—smoothing down along her shoulders in a motion Marcus was beginning to associate with something softer than annoyance.

"We have essence brew," she said. "It's not... caw-fee. But it's warm, and it adjusts to what your body needs." A pause. "Some of the other Earth refugees cried the first time they tasted it."

"I'm not going to cry over a drink."

"We'll see." The grin came back, sharp and knowing. "Come on, Engineer. Let's get you oriented before your guest pass expires. We'll find you a mug on the way."

She turned and kept walking. Marcus followed.

And because the unauthorized channel he'd been tracing, back in the world that now felt like a dream, had shown him something—a pattern in the traffic, not random, not malicious, but *structured*, like language, like protocol, like someone on the other side of reality had been trying to get a message through—he filed that thought away in the part of his brain that never stopped working. Even here. Even now.

Marcus glanced back once, at the spot where he'd arrived, where the stone still held the impression of his body heat.

Then he followed Polly into the impossible, wishing he had a hot cup of coffee and a biscuit for the bird.




Interlude: Polly's Vigil

---

He fell asleep on the archive floor.

Not gracefully. Not the way a mage settles into meditative rest or a traveler finds their bedroll in the dark. Marcus Chen fell asleep the way engineers fall asleep: mid-thought, one hand still reaching for a mug that wasn't there, his body finally overruling a brain that had been running on stale coffee and stubbornness for the better part of two days.

Polly sat on the shelf above him, raven-shaped again because she was comfortable and because nobody was watching, and studied the man the Protocol had delivered.

His breathing had settled into the deep, steady rhythm of genuine unconsciousness. No dreaming yet — that would come later, when the Protocol began its integration work and his sleeping mind tried to process frequencies it had no training for. She'd seen it before. The dreams were never pleasant.

She preened a wing feather. Then another. Methodical. Necessary. The kind of small maintenance that kept her from thinking about the last time she'd sat vigil over someone sleeping on this floor.

*Izack had slept here too. That first night, five hundred and twelve years ago, when the convergence point was still raw and the dimensional membrane was thin enough that the crystal walls hummed with the strain of holding reality together. He'd fallen asleep with chalk in his hair and equations on his hands and a smile she hadn't earned yet.*

She shook the memory away. Feathers resettled.

Marcus Chen. Thirty-two. Systems engineer. Kershaw-Lin corporate security. Three monitors, dead coffee, an apartment he didn't live in. She'd read his Protocol intake assessment while he was still falling through the dimensional membrane, the system presenting his essential characteristics with the clinical precision of a personnel file authored by God.

**Coherence index: 0.92.**

That number had made her sit up on her perch and actually pay attention for the first time in decades.

Ninety-two percent coherence on first contact. Earth-native. No magical training, no attunement exercises, no decades of meditative alignment. Just a man whose declared intent matched his autonomous state with a precision that most Keepers spent their entire careers failing to achieve.

The Protocol's intake assessment had included a behavioral note she'd read three times:

*Subject demonstrates consistent pattern: when confronted with data that contradicts expectation, subject adjusts expectation rather than rejecting data. Adaptation rate: high. Defensive rigidity: low. Threat response: diagnostic rather than combative.*

A security engineer who, when faced with something he couldn't explain, tried to understand it instead of destroying it.

She'd waited five hundred years for that particular combination of traits.

Well. Five hundred and twelve, if she was being precise, which she always was, because precision was the difference between history and nostalgia, and Polly had seen too many good minds dissolve into the latter to tolerate it in herself.

* * *

The crystal archive breathed around them. Not literally — the books hummed, the shelves resonated, the carved symbols on the walls pulsed with the Protocol's 0.3-second heartbeat — but the cumulative effect was respiratory. Inhale: the signatures strengthened, the verification chains tightened, the system confirmed itself. Exhale: a brief loosening, a moment of renewal, the architectural equivalent of blinking.

Marcus didn't notice. Of course he didn't. He was asleep.

But Polly noticed, the way she always noticed, the way she'd been noticing for half a millennium — watching the breath of the system she'd helped build, checking for the stutters and catches that meant something was wrong, the way a parent listens for changes in a sleeping child's breathing without consciously deciding to listen.

The system was tired.

Not failing. Not degraded. Just... carrying weight. The kind of accumulated structural fatigue that came from running at capacity for too long without the maintenance it needed. Kael's ghost-patches were holding, as they always did, because whatever else could be said about the boy, his engineering was extraordinary. But patches were not solutions. Patches were the architectural equivalent of "we'll fix it properly later," and later had been accumulating for forty years.

Polly hopped to the edge of the shelf and looked down at Marcus.

He'd curled onto his side in his sleep, one arm tucked under his head, the other extended toward where his desk would have been if he'd still been in San Francisco. His fingers twitched occasionally — muscle memory reaching for a keyboard that wasn't there. The Protocol's verification light played across his face in slow pulses, painting him in alternating warmth and shadow.

He'd asked about coffee.

That was the thing. That was the thing that had cracked something in her chest that she'd spent decades convincing herself was sealed shut. Not the coherence score. Not the diagnostic instincts. Not the way he'd looked at the impossible and reached for explanations instead of weapons.

He'd asked about coffee.

*"Is it... a weapon?"* she'd asked, and she hadn't been performing ignorance. She'd genuinely wanted to hear how he'd explain the thing he was missing most. What people chose to mourn told you everything about what they loved, and Marcus Chen, dropped through the floor of reality into a world that shouldn't exist, running a seventy-two-hour clock on his continued existence, had mourned a mediocre cup of coffee.

Not power. Not status. Not his apartment or his career or his reflection in a mirror. A warm drink and the ritual of holding it.

*Izack had mourned chalk. The first week in the convergence space, before Avalon had walls or a name, he'd patted his pockets seventeen times looking for chalk that wasn't there, because chalk meant he could write equations, and equations meant the world was still something you could think about clearly.*

*They were the same, in the ways that mattered. Engineers who loved their tools because the tools meant they could keep working. People whose grief was practical rather than philosophical.*

*And Polly had loved them both on sight, which was a problem she'd been managing for five hundred years and apparently hadn't solved.*

She ruffled her feathers — not the storm-flatten of distress or the slow smoothing of comfort, but the full-body shake that meant she was resettling something internal. Resetting. Deciding.

The last Architect had bound himself into the system's substrate to hold a gap closed that the six tongues couldn't govern. He was still down there, in the crystal observatory, smoking Fizzlecress and holding twenty-one dimensions together through sheer bloody-minded refusal to stop caring about calibration. She brought him tea. He didn't look up.

That was fine. That was architecture. Architecture was sacrifice shaped like a building, and Izack had always been better at building than at staying.

But Marcus wasn't Izack.

Marcus was the thing Izack had built a back door for: someone who could see the system from outside it, read its failures without being compromised by its assumptions, and care enough about coherence to ask the questions the system couldn't ask itself.

*What do the dead need from the living?*

*What happens to the things the system drops?*

*Where do endings go when there's no governance path for ending?*

Marcus had been in Aethermoor for less than six hours, and he'd already asked the question that five hundred years of Keepers had managed not to formulate. Not because they were stupid or negligent, but because the system they served had no vocabulary for it. You can't ask about the thing your language has no word for.

Unless you speak a different language entirely.

Unless you come from a world where systems crash and the error logs tell you why, where processes terminate and the operating system keeps running, where death is not an architectural impossibility but a Tuesday.

* * *

Below them, deep in the substrate, the Seventh Holding shifted.

Polly felt it the way she always felt it — not as sound or vibration but as a change in the quality of the archive's silence. A slight densening. A gathering. As if the ghosts in the holding had sensed Marcus's presence and were paying attention the way the rest of the system was paying attention.

*Not yet,* she thought at them. *Give him time. He doesn't know what he is yet.*

The holding subsided. Patient. It had been patient for a very long time.

Marcus's fingers twitched again, reaching for a keyboard or a coffee mug or the last stable thing he remembered, and Polly felt something shift in her own architecture — not magical, not systemic, but personal. The thing she'd sealed shut after Izack descended. The thing she'd maintained as a controlled absence in her own governance, the way you leave a shelf empty for a book that hasn't been written yet.

Hope.

She hated it. Hope was the most dangerous state in her emotional taxonomy, worse than grief, worse than rage, because hope made you build load-bearing structures on foundations that might not hold. Hope made you invest in futures that could collapse. Hope made you care about someone who had seventy-two hours to establish a permanent baseline and might not make it.

But she'd been doing this for five hundred years, and the Protocol hadn't sent anyone else, and Marcus Chen had asked about coffee, and Polly's relationship with hope was complicated in exactly the ways that mattered.

She hopped down from the shelf. Landed on the floor with the soft click of talons on crystal. Walked over to where Marcus was sleeping and settled herself approximately six inches from his outstretched hand, close enough to be present, far enough to maintain the professional distance that the situation required.

Tucked her head under her wing.

Made the small, involuntary sound that she would deny under oath was a sleep-caw but that anyone who'd spent time around ravens would recognize immediately: the soft, raspy vocalization of a bird who has decided that this particular spot, next to this particular person, is safe enough to close her eyes.

The archive hummed around them.

The Protocol pulsed its steady heartbeat.

And somewhere in the deep substrate, a tired man in a crystal chair felt something he hadn't felt in decades — a change in the system's emotional weather, as subtle as the difference between a room where someone is sleeping alone and a room where someone is sleeping next to someone who chose to stay.

Izack didn't look up from his work.

But his pipe smoke curled a fraction more gently, and the seven corrupted facets of the observatory wall flickered, just for a moment, toward a color that might have been called hope if anyone had been there to name it.

Nobody was.

Polly slept.

Marcus dreamed of coffee.

The system breathed.

---

*Keeper's note, unlogged: He asked about coffee. That's enough. That's enough to start.*




Chapter 2: The Language Barrier

The corridor tasted like static electricity and old libraries.

Marcus noticed this because his senses were doing something they had never done before—overlapping. The crystallized walls threw off a low hum that he could *feel* on his tongue, a fizzing mineral tang that sat right behind his molars. The light, sourceless and diffuse, had a texture: silk, maybe, or the surface of still water just before you touch it. Even the air carried information in a register he didn't have a name for—not smell, exactly, not temperature, but something adjacent to both.

Polly walked ahead of him in her humanoid shape, wings folded tight against her back, feathers catching the not-quite-light and throwing back colors that shouldn't exist in the visible spectrum. She'd stayed in this form since the library—*hands are useful*, she'd said, and apparently so were legs that covered more ground than a raven's hop. She moved with the casual efficiency of someone who'd navigated these corridors so many times the act had become autonomic—feet finding the right path the way a pianist's fingers find keys, without conscious thought, through muscle memory so deep it was closer to instinct.

"The Archives exist in protocol space," she said over her shoulder, turning down a passage that Marcus was certain had not been there three seconds ago. "Physical location is negotiable. What matters is authorization level."

"Negotiable how?"

"The same room can be in six places at once, depending on who's walking through it. My version of this corridor has seventeen doors. Yours has three. Someone at First Circle sees a hallway that goes on forever." She paused at an intersection where three paths converged on a point that, from a Euclidean perspective, shouldn't have been able to hold them. "It's not that the space is bigger on the inside. It's that the space is *differently addressed* for each observer. Like—" She turned to him, searching for the right analogy, feathers ruffling in thought.

"Like virtual memory pages mapped to different process spaces," Marcus said.

Polly blinked. Both eyelids—the outer ones and an inner translucent pair he hadn't noticed before. "Yes. Exactly like that. Whatever that means."

"It means I'm starting to understand why the Protocol recruited an engineer."

"Recruited is a strong word." She started walking again. "Requisitioned, maybe. Conscripted. Downloaded against your will." A beat. "Welcome aboard."

They passed through a section where the crystal walls were older—darker, rougher, carved rather than grown. The shelves here held volumes bound in something that wasn't leather, wasn't metal, but had the weight and permanence of both. Marcus ran his fingers along the spines as they passed. Each one vibrated at a slightly different frequency, and his new cross-wired senses translated those vibrations into colors, into temperatures, into the ghost-impressions of meanings he almost grasped.

"Don't touch those," Polly said without turning around. "Those are pre-Founding archives. The encoding is... unstable."

"Pre-Founding?"

"Before the Architect built the current system." She said it the way engineers on Earth said *before we migrated to the cloud*—reverent, slightly terrified, mostly grateful they hadn't been there for it. "The old protocols were raw. Unstructured. Powerful, but—" She searched for a word. "Hungry. They ate context instead of preserving it. The Architect gave us the Six Tongues as a governance layer. Before that, magic was..." She trailed off, and her feathers did something Marcus hadn't seen before: they pressed flat against her skull, tight and protective, the way a bird's plumage slicks down in a storm. "Magic was a conversation where both sides could lie."

Marcus filed that away. The Architect. Pre-Founding. Hungry protocols. He was building a mental model, and every piece Polly dropped—deliberately or not—snapped into place with the satisfying click of a well-designed API. Someone had *built* this system. Someone had looked at the raw chaos of whatever magic was and said: *we need architecture*.

An engineer, he thought. The Architect was an engineer.

"Is the Architect still—"

"Alive?" Polly's feathers ruffled. "Yes. Busy? Perpetually. Available for questions?" She made a noise that was either a laugh or a warning. "Izack Thorne has not been available for questions in the way normal people mean the word 'available' for a very long time. He is... deeper than he used to be. You may meet him eventually. Or you may meet what he has become. The distinction is increasingly academic."

She kept walking before Marcus could ask what that meant.

"Here." Polly stopped at what appeared to be a blank section of wall. She pressed her palm flat against the crystal, and Marcus watched her wings extend slightly—not a stretch, but an antenna deployment. The feathers along her leading edges vibrated at a frequency he could see as a thin line of gold, and the wall responded. Not by opening. By *becoming*.

A door crystallized out of the surface, edges sharpening from blur to definition like a photograph developing in real time. The door was tall—twelve feet at least—and covered in inscriptions that Marcus's universal translation rendered not as words but as *relationships*. Mathematical relationships, recursive and nested, each symbol containing its own definition and the definition of its relationship to every other symbol on the door.

"Self-documenting code," Marcus breathed.

Polly's obsidian eyes cut to him. "You keep doing that."

"Doing what?"

"Seeing the right thing." She said it like it was slightly dangerous. Like noticing the right pattern too quickly could attract the wrong kind of attention. Then the door opened, and Marcus forgot about danger, forgot about caution, forgot about the taste of static on his tongue and the three-hours-cold coffee he'd never finished and the apartment in the Sunset District where an expired yogurt was slowly becoming a science experiment.

The chamber beyond was a cathedral.

Not metaphorically. Not in the diluted sense of *large impressive room*. This space had the proportions, the acoustics, the gravitational *presence* of a structure built to make human beings feel the exact size they actually were in the context of everything else. The ceiling soared upward in a spiraling dome of crystal facets, each one catching and refracting the sourceless light into cascading spectra that painted the air itself with color. Along the lower walls, crystal formations grew in shapes that were almost botanical — branching, flowering, heavy with luminescence the way trees are heavy with fruit. Marcus's detection faculty, which he did not yet know he had, registered them as alive. Not decorative. *Alive*, the way a library is alive when the books in it are still being read. Rivers of pale light ran through channels in the floor, converging and separating like thought made visible. The whole space felt less like it had been built and more like it had been *grown* by something that loved knowledge the way gardeners love soil.

The floor was a mosaic of dark stone and pale crystal, arranged in a hexagonal pattern that repeated at every scale—the same shape in the tiles, in the arrangement of tiles, in the arrangement of arrangements, fractals all the way down.

And at the center, six pedestals.

Each one carved from a different mineral, arranged in a perfect hexagon, each holding a sphere of crystallized light that pulsed with its own rhythm, its own color, its own *voice*. Because Marcus could hear them now. Not with his ears—with whatever new sense this place had woken in him. Six voices, singing. Not in harmony. In *counterpoint*. Each one distinct, each one complete in itself, each one aware of the others and choosing, moment by moment, how to relate.

Polly stood at the threshold and let him look.

"The Six Sacred Tongues," she said, and for the first time since he'd met her, the sarcasm was completely gone. Her voice carried something older. Reverence, yes, but also familiarity—the way you speak about a place you've loved so long that loving it has become indistinguishable from breathing. "Each one a domain of intent. To speak them is to mean things at the protocol level."

Marcus stepped into the chamber. The hexagonal floor tiles responded to his weight—not physically, but *informationally*. He could feel the system registering his presence, routing his guest-pass authentication through layers he couldn't see, checking and re-checking that he was allowed to be here. The 0.3-second heartbeat Polly had described in the corridor—he could feel it now, a pulse in his sternum that wasn't his actual heartbeat but ran alongside it like a second clock.

He approached the first pedestal.

**KO.** Kor'aelin. The sphere pulsed red-gold, steady and commanding, and the sound it made—the sound his new sense translated from vibration into meaning—was *authority*. Not the brittle authority of someone shouting orders. The deep authority of a foundation. Of the ground beneath your feet. Of the statement *this is what should be true*. The frequency sat at the overlap with concert pitch A, and Marcus's musician's ear (he'd played bass in a college jazz band, badly, for two semesters) recognized it as the note orchestras tune to. The reference point. The beginning.

**AV.** Avali. Blue-silver and quick, flowing like water finding its path downhill. This one didn't pulse so much as *stream*—a continuous movement of light within the sphere, tracing routes and channels and pathways. Transport. The how-to-get-there tongue. Marcus thought of TCP handshakes, of SYN-ACK packets, of the invisible infrastructure that makes connection possible. The frequency sat in a soprano's middle register, and it made him think of messengers. Of roads. Of the space between intention and arrival.

**RU.** Runethic. Deep purple, and the pulse here was different—not a rhythm but a *weight*. Each beat landed like a gavel. This was the policy tongue, and Marcus could feel it in his teeth: *who is allowed*. *What is permitted*. *Under what conditions*. The inscriptions on the chamber door had been written in RU, he realized. Self-documenting access control. Every magical artifact in this world, Polly would tell him later, carried RU inscriptions defining its permissions. Reality itself ran on access control lists.

**CA.** Cassisivadan. White-gold and *complex*—the sphere didn't pulse so much as *compute*. Layers of light folding over each other, interference patterns creating and destroying structure in continuous cascading transformation. This was the math tongue. The encryption tongue. The tongue that turned raw energy into specific outcomes. Of all six, this one felt most like home to Marcus. This was where the engineering happened.

**UM.** Umbroth. Shadow-black, and this was the one that made his skin prickle. Because the sphere *shone*—darkness that somehow emitted anti-light, a photonic negative that his eyes kept trying to resolve and failing. This was the security tongue. The privacy tongue. The tongue of spies and assassins and anyone who needed to make something *not there*. Its frequency nested inside Avali's range like a hidden channel—a steganographic whisper buried in legitimate traffic.

**DR.** Draumric. Earth-brown, and the lowest frequency of all. The foundation. Marcus felt this one in his feet, through the stone floor, through the bones of his legs. This was the schema tongue—structure, authentication, data integrity. Every official document, every identity token, every binding contract in Aethermoor was written in DR. If KO was *what should be true* and CA was *how to make it true*, DR was *proof that it is true*. The signature layer. The receipt.

Six tongues. Six domains. Six voices singing in counterpoint, and Marcus Chen stood at the center of their hexagon and felt, for the first time since falling through the floor of reality, something that wasn't confusion or fear or the stubborn engineer's refusal to accept the impossible.

He felt *recognition*.

Not of the tongues themselves. He'd never seen them, never heard them, never known they existed until twelve minutes ago when a raven in a graduation cap had promised to explain them over a drink he hadn't yet received. But the *pattern*. The architecture. The way six separate concerns were isolated into their own domains, each one self-contained but aware of the others, communicating through well-defined interfaces—

He'd spent his career building systems like this. He'd just never seen one that ran reality.

"Can I touch one?" he asked.

Polly had been watching him from the doorway, arms crossed, head tilted at that corvid angle. "Sure. Won't do much—you're not authorized yet."

Marcus reached for the KO sphere.

The moment his fingers made contact, the chamber disappeared.

Not gradually. Not like the white void of his arrival, which had dissolved the world in stages. This was instantaneous—reality replaced wholesale by something else. One frame he was standing in a crystal cathedral with his hand on a glowing sphere. The next frame he was—

—*inside the system*.

He saw himself. Not reflected, not mirrored. *Rendered*. Marcus Chen: a data structure, floating in a space that had no physical dimensions but infinite informational ones. His guest-pass authentication blinked in amber at the edge of his awareness—PENDING, PENDING, PENDING—while sixteen threads spun out from his core, each one probing a different verification layer, each one returning the same confused result: *entity type unrecognized. Earth-native. No baseline template found. Constructing provisional profile...*

And beneath the threads, he glimpsed something deeper. His state vector—nine dimensions of data the Protocol tracked continuously. Not physical dimensions. *Semantic* dimensions. His context window (everything he was currently perceiving). His temporal phase (the shape of his intent as it formed across time). His entropy level (how ordered or chaotic his thoughts were). His quantum coherence (how stable his existence remained from one 0.3-second heartbeat to the next). Nine numbers, updated in real time, defining not just whether he existed but *how well* he existed.

The Protocol's shorthand for *is this entity worth keeping?*

Then, from beneath it all, rising through the layers like a whale surfacing: the question.

***What do you intend?***

It arrived the same way it had during his transit—not as words, not as thought, but as a fundamental condition. Like being asked your name by gravity itself. Marcus felt his complex, tangled, contradictory human intent—I want to understand, I want to go home, I want to not die, I want to know why I'm here, I want coffee, I want to UNDERSTAND—compress. All the imaginary components, the doubts and fears and half-formed impulses, stripped away. What remained was a single real-valued vector. Clean. Honest. Irreducible.

*I intend to understand.*

The sphere pulsed.

For one heartbeat—one actual heartbeat, his biological one, synced for a single moment with the Protocol's 0.3-second tick—Marcus felt *recognized*. Not known. Not understood. *Recognized*. The way a system recognizes a valid input. The way a lock recognizes its key. A fundamental mechanical acceptance: *you are a thing that belongs in the category of things I process*.

Then the connection severed, and he was back in the cathedral, stumbling backward, gasping, his hand burning with phantom heat that was already fading.

"Congratulations," Polly said, catching his elbow with a grip that was warmer and steadier than her voice. "You just completed your first Protocol handshake."

Marcus leaned on her, breathing hard. The chamber spun. The six spheres sang their counterpoint, indifferent to his existential crisis. "What... was that?"

"Intent verification. The Protocol needs to know you're not a rogue process before it grants deeper access." She steered him toward a bench carved from the base of the DR pedestal—the schema tongue, the foundation, solid and grounding beneath him. "Sit. Breathe."

He sat. He breathed. His engineer brain, bless its relentless pattern-matching heart, was already trying to taxonomize the experience. *Classification: first-contact handshake protocol. Method: direct neural injection of semantic query. Response format: dimensionally-reduced intent vector. Authentication result: provisional accept.*

"Usually takes people three tries to even get a response from the spheres," Polly said, settling onto the bench beside him. Her wings folded forward slightly, creating a loose canopy over his shoulders—not quite an embrace, but a shelter. He suspected she did it unconsciously. "The Tongues like you."

"The Tongues are sentient?"

"The Tongues are *infrastructure*." She said it the way a priest might say *God is love*—with absolute conviction and a faint awareness that the statement was doing a lot of heavy lifting. "They don't think. They don't want. But they resonate with certain patterns of intent more than others, and yours—" She studied him with those obsidian eyes. "Yours is unusually clean. Most Earth refugees carry so much noise in their intent that the Protocol can barely parse them. Fear. Denial. Grief. You have all of that, obviously, but underneath it there's this... thread. This clear, persistent signal." She tapped her temple. "You *want to understand*. Not escape. Not survive. *Understand*. That's rare."

Marcus didn't know what to say to that. On Earth, wanting to understand things had mostly gotten him called *intense* by dates who didn't call back and *obsessive* by managers who gave him the hardest problems because they knew he wouldn't stop until the system made sense. It had never been described as a virtue before.

"Okay," he said, because silence was making the impossibility louder again. "Tell me about the Tongues."

Polly counted on her too-long fingers.

"KO. Kor'aelin. The Control Tongue." She nodded toward the red-gold sphere. "High-level declarations. *I want this to happen*. When you cast a spell—when anyone casts anything, from a light charm to a continental ward—you're speaking KO to tell reality what outcome you're requesting. Not how. Not why. Just *what*."

"Like a function call with no implementation."

"I don't know what that means, but your face did the right thing, so yes." She moved to the next finger. "AV. Avali. The Transport Tongue. This is the *how to get there*. Initialization, routing, channel establishment. If KO says *teleport me to the castle*, AV says *here's the secure path through protocol space to make that happen*."

"TCP handshake."

"Again, face did the thing. Trust the face." Third finger. "RU. Runethic. The Policy Tongue. Access control. Permissions. *Who is allowed to do what, under what conditions, with what exceptions*. Every artifact in Aethermoor carries RU inscriptions. Every locked door, every restricted zone, every—" She gestured at the chamber around them. "This room is RU-gated. Your guest pass includes a temporary RU clearance to be here. Without it, you'd walk right past the wall and never know the door existed."

"Authorization middleware."

"I'm going to start charging you for translations." Fourth finger. "CA. Cassisivadan. The Compute Tongue. Encryption, transformation, processing. This is where the heavy lifting happens. Energy conversion, transmutation, complex spellwork. If magic has a math department, it speaks CA."

Marcus nodded at the white-gold sphere. "That one felt like home."

"It would. CA is the engineer's tongue." Something flickered in Polly's expression—there and gone, like a fish surfacing and diving. Not quite sadness. More like... recognition of a pattern she'd seen before. In someone else. A long time ago.

"Fifth." She pressed on before Marcus could ask. "UM. Umbroth. The Security Tongue. Privacy, concealment, protection. Making things invisible, undetectable, unfindable. Spies and assassins train for years in UM. It's the most dangerous tongue to learn, because the better you get at hiding things from the Protocol, the harder the Protocol works to find you." A pause. "And the Protocol always wins. Eventually."

"Security through obscurity is no security at all."

"Now you're quoting the Architect." She said it casually, but her feathers did the storm-flatten again, just for a moment.

"The Architect said that?"

"The Architect said a lot of things." Polly's voice was carefully neutral. "Most of them are carved into the walls of the Deep Library. Last tongue. DR. Draumric. The Schema Tongue. Structure, authentication, data integrity. This is the signature layer. Every official document, every identity token, every binding contract—DR makes it real. If KO is the intent and CA is the computation, DR is the *proof*. The tamper-evident seal that says *this happened, and here's the cryptographic evidence*."

Marcus stared at the six spheres, their voices weaving through the chamber in endless counterpoint. Six domains. Six concerns. Six layers of a system that ran the fundamental operations of reality itself.

"This is a network stack," he said quietly.

Not a question. A recognition.

Polly watched him, her head tilted, feathers smoothing back to their resting state. The grin came back—wide and sharp and genuine. "There it is."

"No, I mean—" Marcus stood, turning to take in the whole hexagon. "This is *literally* a network stack. KO is the application layer. AV is the transport layer. RU is the access control layer. CA is the computation layer. UM is the security layer. DR is the data integrity layer. You've got a complete protocol suite running reality as a *service*."

"Welcome to Aethermoor, Marcus." Polly spread her wings slightly, and the cathedral light caught them, and for one moment the chamber was all dark feathers and prismatic refraction and six voices singing the architecture of the world. "Where magic is engineering and engineering is magic."

Marcus sat back down on the DR bench. His hands had stopped shaking, he realized. Somewhere between the Protocol handshake and the network stack revelation, his body had made a decision his mind hadn't caught up to yet: *this is real, and I'm going to work the problem*.

"Okay," he said. "How do I learn them?"

Polly's grin softened into something warmer. "One at a time. Starting tomorrow. Tonight, you rest." She stood, wings resettling against her back. "And I owe you a drink."

"The essence brew?"

"You remembered." She sounded surprised, and a little pleased. "Come on. The refectory is three doors east—or west, depending on your clearance level. For you, it's east."

She led him out of the cathedral, past the self-documenting door that dissolved behind them like a browser tab closing, back into the corridors of negotiable space.

The route to the refectory took them across an open sky-bridge — a span of translucent crystal arcing between two sections of the Spire that Marcus hadn't realized were separate structures. Wind hit him. Real wind, warm and strange, carrying the scent of rain on stone and something floral he had no name for.

He looked down.

The Spire wasn't a tower. It was the center of a world.

Below the bridge, the terrain opened in concentric rings like tree growth — gardens, then groves, then open land stretching toward a horizon that curved wrong, too close in some directions, too far in others, as if the pocket dimension's geometry was still making up its mind about scale. A river of pale azure light wound between the rings, not flowing downhill but following some invisible current that had nothing to do with gravity. Where it passed near gardens, the plants grew toward it like sunflowers tracking the sun, except these tracked meaning instead of light.

Farther out, mountain peaks drifted at varying altitudes, some trailing waterfalls that fell sideways before curving upward into mist. Others carried entire meadows on their shoulders, green and impossible, livestock-sized shapes moving across them in patterns too regular to be random and too organic to be programmed.

*This is not a campus. This is a continent someone dreamed into existence and then kept dreaming.*

A formation of what looked like giant dragonflies — translucent wings catching the aurora light, each one large enough to carry a rider — banked across the middle distance, heading toward a cluster of floating islands connected by bridges of living vine.

"The Dream Gardens," Polly said, following his gaze. "Below that, the Leyline Nexus. Don't go there without clearance. Don't go to the Mind-Forest at all until you can control your surface thoughts." She paused. "Izack built this place over centuries. Started as a single island in a pocket dimension he anchored to his watch. Grew it ring by ring, ley by ley, like a coral reef made of intention. Some of the outer rings are still growing. Some of the inner ones are older than most civilizations on your world."

*He built this. One person built this. Out of nothing. Out of imagination.*

Marcus stared at the impossible geography until Polly tugged his sleeve.

"Food first. Existential vertigo second. You'll have years to gawk at the scenery if you survive the next three days."

Marcus followed, his mind still running hot, still pattern-matching, still building the mental model that was going to keep him alive.

Six tongues. Seventy-two hours. One guest pass running down like a timer on a bomb he couldn't see.

But he had a framework now. And Marcus Chen, for all his flaws—the expired yogurt, the abandoned apartment, the sixteen-hour shifts and the dates that never called back—was very, very good with frameworks.

Behind him, in the cathedral, the six spheres continued their song. And if Marcus had looked back, he might have noticed something he'd missed in his first sweep of the chamber: a seventh pedestal, empty, standing slightly apart from the hexagon. Darker than the others. Older. Carved with inscriptions in a script that none of the six tongues could parse.

Marcus didn't look back at the seventh pedestal, though part of him wanted to.

Ahead of him: coffee, sleep, and a world where closing your eyes meant trusting the Protocol to keep verifying you while your conscious mind went offline.

He would learn, later, to be afraid of that.




Chapter 3: Hyperbolic Consequences

The first drink in Aethermoor arrived in a stone mug the size of a flowerpot and tasted like someone had reverse-engineered comfort from first principles.

It was hot. It was bitter. It had that dark, roasted edge coffee should have, plus something he had never found in any cafe on Earth: a precise, almost rude awareness of what his body was missing. Salt, maybe. Glucose. Dignity.

Marcus held the mug with both hands and breathed steam while his nervous system tried to remember what "not currently in mortal onboarding" felt like.

"Essence brew," Polly said, watching him over the lip of her own cup. She was back in raven form now, perched on the table like she paid taxes here. Graduation cap at a slight angle, monocle polished, bowtie immaculate. "It calibrates to need-state."

"Need-state?"

"What your system is low on. Electrolytes. Baseline calories. Emotional stability." She tilted her head, one obsidian eye tracking his face with corvid precision. "For you, apparently, ninety percent caffeine and unresolved sarcasm."

Marcus took another sip and closed his eyes.

"I hate that this is good," he said.

"You drank dead office coffee that had entered its sedimentary era," Polly said. "This was always going to clear that bar."

He opened one eye at her. "You don't even have lips."

"And yet here we are, me being right with my entire beak."

The refectory was smaller than the cathedral chamber, warmer too, built from the same crystal-stone geometry but shaped for bodies that needed food and sleep instead of revelation. Long tables. Hanging lamps that hummed at a frequency his teeth could feel. A service counter where nobody was visible, but trays kept arriving exactly when people needed them.

People. Not many, but enough to make Marcus feel the shape of the larger system around him. A woman with silver tattoos moving up her neck like circuit traces, speaking softly to a bowl of glowing broth. A teenager in a patched flight jacket arguing with his own spoon in what sounded like three languages at once. An older man in work gloves asleep upright, head on folded arms, a DR-sealed wristband pulsing amber every third heartbeat.

"Refugees?" Marcus asked.

Polly nodded. "Most of them. Aether-born staff mixed in. Keepers, runners, medics."

"Everyone looks..."

"Tired?"

"Like they passed a test they didn't study for."

Polly gave a low, approving caw into her mug. "That's the official orientation mood."

Marcus took a longer drink and felt warmth push down into places that had been clenched since the white void.

*Okay,* he told himself. *New world. New protocol stack. Try not to die before breakfast.*

"How long until training starts?" he asked.

"Training started when you touched KO and didn't explode." Polly hopped off the table, landed with a neat click of talons, then looked up at him. "Formal lesson in seven minutes. Drink."

"You're very bossy for a bird wearing graduation cosplay."

"I'm very old for a man who still thinks five minutes of sleep debt is a personality." She twitched one wing. "Move."

* * *

The route from the refectory to the training hall took them across an open terrace that Marcus had not braced for.

Morning had arrived in Avalon — if morning was even the word. The sky above was not a sky in any sense he recognized. It was layered: an inner dome of soft gold light closest to the Spire, then rings of deeper color stretching outward — indigo, emerald, the faint blush of something between sunset and perpetual dawn. Leyline rivers cut glowing paths through the landscape below, some of them curving upward along cliff faces before disappearing into clouds that drifted sideways at walking speed.

*Valleys that swoop into rivers. Rivers that climb waterfalls and keep going. Mountains floating at altitudes that change while you watch.*

A grove of enormous trees grew along the nearest ring of terrain, their trunks wrapped in living runes that pulsed like slow heartbeats. Between them, smaller paths wound through gardens where the plants appeared to be having conversations — leaves turning toward each other, vines withdrawing from shadow and reaching for the leyline glow. One tree had a face. Not carved. Grown. It looked mildly annoyed, which Marcus found relatable.

Somewhere in the middle distance, a creature the size of a delivery van drifted between two floating meadows, its translucent wings catching the light like stained glass. A herd of something woolly grazed on a boulder that hovered thirty feet above a canyon, completely unbothered by the absence of ground.

*This man built a planet inside his imagination and then the planet kept growing after he left. That's not architecture. That's parenthood.*

"Marcus," Polly said from the terrace railing. "Training hall. Now."

He tore his eyes away from the impossible geography and followed her inside.

The training hall was a circle pretending to be a square.

Marcus knew that sentence made no sense. He also knew it was true.

From the doorway it looked like a standard room: four walls, flat floor, high ceiling, no furniture except a single obsidian plinth in the center. Three steps in, the corners softened. Five steps in, they retreated — pulling away like the room was inhaling. By the time he reached the plinth, he could still see four walls, but the lines between them no longer agreed on distance, and the floor had developed the unsettling conviction that downhill was a matter of perspective.

"Do not trust Euclidean instincts in here," Polly said. She had shifted humanoid again for instruction mode, wings folded, sleeves rolled to the elbow. "This chamber is mapped to a local hyperbolic manifold. Intent drift is visually amplified so apprentices can see their own errors before the errors become expensive."

Marcus stared at the far wall. It looked forty feet away until he focused on it, then sixty, then twelve.

"You're teaching me geometry with threat displays," he said.

"I am teaching you consequences with geometry."

Polly tapped the plinth. Six symbols ignited around its rim—KO, AV, RU, CA, UM, DR—in a dim, waiting glow.

"Today you do one thing," she said. "A controlled KO declaration. No casting chain. No transport. No policy override. Just intent expression in clean form."

"Like a function signature."

"Exactly. KO is declaration, not implementation."

Marcus put his hands in his pockets to keep them from fidgeting. "What am I declaring?"

"Simple target. There." Polly pointed to a brass ring mounted on a stand across the room. "You will declare: *open.*"

"Open what?"

"The ring."

"What does an open ring do?"

"Right now? Nothing dramatic. It transitions from closed-state to pass-state. Think... gateway primitive."

"That sounds dramatic in a different font."

"Marcus."

"Right. Open ring. No explosions."

He walked to the marked line on the floor. There was no literal line, but the chamber made one anyway—a thin seam of pale light at his feet.

"Focus your intent," Polly said. "Not your words. Words are a convenience layer."

"Convenience layer in a system that asks moral geometry questions every point-three seconds."

"Welcome to premium service."

Marcus exhaled. Tried to clear his head. Failed. Tried again.

His brain offered a helpful slideshow of everything currently wrong: alien world, countdown to existential deletion, missing apartment keys, stale coffee left on his desk, unknown HR implications of dimensional transfer.

*I intend to open the ring,* he thought.

Nothing.

"Again," Polly said.

*I intend to open the ring.*

The brass ring wobbled, flashed once, and the obsidian plinth coughed out a wooden chair.

The chair was extremely real, aggressively ordinary, and placed exactly one inch in front of Marcus's knees.

He sat down by accident.

Polly covered her face with one hand.

"Did I just summon furniture?"

"You declared *open* while internally intending 'please let me sit down before I black out.'" Polly dragged her hand down her face. "The system believed your honest signal."

Marcus looked at the chair. "I hate that this is fair."

"This is why we train. Unclean intent in KO leaks into outcomes. Best case, accidental chair. Worst case, accidental catastrophe with good grammar."

He stood, moved the chair aside, and reset on the line.

"Again," Polly said.

This time he narrowed his internal world to one object and one action. Brass ring. Open state.

*I intend to open the ring.*

The ring split along an invisible seam, rotating thirty degrees with a soft metallic click. A narrow aperture appeared in its center, filled with amber light.

"Good," Polly said. "Hold it for three heartbeats."

He held. The aperture stayed stable. His sternum ticked with the now-familiar secondary pulse—Protocol heartbeat overlaying his own.

On the fourth beat, the room dimmed.

An RU glyph flared above the ring in red.

DENIED: GUEST PASS SCOPE EXCEEDED.

The aperture snapped shut.

Marcus's heart stuttered.

Not metaphorically. His actual heartbeat — the organic one, the sixty-eight-beats-per-minute rhythm he'd carried since birth — skipped. The Protocol's verification pulse kept going at its immovable 0.3 seconds, but his body's rhythm fell out of sync with it, and the gap between the two beats opened like a crack in a floor he'd been standing on.

His vision blurred. Not dramatically — just a softening at the edges, as if the room had decided he was slightly less real than he'd been three seconds ago. The crystal walls, which had been sharp and detailed, went flat. Like a game rendering at low resolution because the GPU was saving power for something more important.

*Guest pass,* Marcus thought. *That's what running on a guest pass feels like from the inside. Not a warning. Not a denial. Just... less resolution. The system allocating fewer resources to verifying my existence because I pushed past what I'm authorized to be.*

His knees buckled. He caught himself on the chair — the accidental one, still there, still aggressively ordinary — and sat down hard.

"Breathe," Polly said. Her voice was different. Not sarcastic. Clear, close, the voice of someone who had seen this before and knew exactly how bad it could get. "Your baseline is destabilizing. The guest pass is recalibrating. It does that when you exceed scope — it doesn't punish you, it just... forgets to render you as carefully for a few seconds."

"That's punishment."

"That's resource allocation. Punishment would be letting you flicker."

Marcus breathed. In. Out. His heartbeat found the Protocol's pulse again — not matching it, never matching it, but running close enough that the system apparently decided he was worth the rendering budget. The walls sharpened. His hands stopped shaking. The crack in the floor closed.

"How close was I to flickering?" he asked.

Polly was quiet for one beat too long.

"Close enough that I moved closer," she said. And Marcus realized she had — she was on the table edge nearest him now, six inches from his arm, her feathers pressed flat in the configuration he was learning meant *I am concerned and would rather eat glass than admit it.*

Something about her proximity helped. Not magically — or maybe magically, he couldn't tell anymore — but his baseline steadied faster with her nearby. As if the system verified him more confidently when another verified entity was vouching for his continued relevance through the simple act of choosing to sit close.

"So," Marcus said, when his voice worked again. "I passed and failed at the same time."

"You passed KO. You failed RU. Different layer, different gate."

Marcus let out a short laugh that came out halfway to a groan.

"Your magic stack has better error messages than most enterprise software."

"Because people die when logs are vague."

Silence landed between them for one beat too long.

Polly softened first, feathers settling at her shoulders.

"You did well," she said. "First-day apprentices usually produce either sparks or crying. You produced a coherent declaration, one accidental chair, and a policy denial. That's a respectable morning."

"Please never put that on my performance review."

"No promises."

She tapped the plinth again. The symbols around the rim brightened, then dimmed in sequence KO-AV-RU-CA-UM-DR.

"Now watch," she said.

She stepped to the line, closed her eyes, and made no visible gesture.

The chamber flexed.

Marcus felt it before he saw it: distance changing as a function of something he could not name. The brass ring seemed to slide farther away while also becoming larger. The walls bowed outward. The floor beneath him remained flat but started *feeling* steep.

"Tiny intent drift," Polly said calmly, eyes still closed, "multiplied through harmonic cost." A second ring appeared beside the first, then a third, each one slightly misaligned. "In bounded systems, drift is annoying. In hyperbolic systems, drift compounds."

The third ring shuddered, failed to stabilize, and dissolved into light.

Polly opened her eyes. The room snapped back to baseline.

"You don't get to be vague here," she said. "Vagueness is expensive."

Marcus stared at the empty space where the third ring had been.

"So the world is punitive toward sloppy thinking."

"The world is literal toward sloppy thinking. Punishment is just what literals feel like from the inside."

He folded that sentence into the part of his mind labeled *things to unpack after sleep and maybe two more mugs of calibrated existential soup.*

"Okay," he said. "What do I do until RU stops yelling at me?"

"Build baseline coherence. Learn controlled declarations. Don't lie to yourself about your own intent." Polly tilted her head, sharp and birdlike even in human shape. "And sleep. Sleep is not optional for new arrivals."

Marcus gave her a look.

"If you say "sleep hygiene" I'm leaving."

"I was going to say "sleep or cease.""

"Your bedside manner is terrifying."

"My bedside results are excellent."

* * *

The sleeping ward looked like a chapel designed by someone who trusted encryption more than prayer.

Narrow alcoves lined the walls, each with a low bed, a folded blanket, and a DR sigil engraved above the headrest. The air was cool and quiet. Not silent—never silent, not in Aethermoor—but padded. Buffered. The background hum was lower here, tuned to something close to a heartbeat at rest.

Polly stopped at an empty alcove and handed Marcus a thin wrist cuff made of braided metal and dark thread.

"Wear this while you sleep."

"Tracking bracelet?"

"Stability tether."

"Difference?"

"One keeps you from leaving a geofence. The other keeps reality from dropping your session while you're unconscious."

Marcus slipped it on. The cuff tightened one notch, then warmed to skin temperature.

"Comforting," he said. "Nothing says "sweet dreams" like "anti-ontological disconnect hardware.""

"I can add a lullaby if you want."

"Can it be not terrifying?"

"No."

He sat on the bed. The mattress was firmer than expected, maybe by design. A subtle KO glyph pulsed once over the alcove entrance, then faded.

Polly leaned against the frame, arms crossed.

"Before you crash," she said, "you need one anchor phrase. Something true. Short. Repeatable."

"Like a mantra?"

"Like a checksum for your own head."

Marcus considered. The room smelled faintly of clean linen and mineral dust and the last trace of essence brew on his breath.

*What do you intend?*

The Protocol's question surfaced again, not as panic this time, but as structure.

He looked at Polly.

"I intend to understand," he said. "And stay."

For a moment her expression did that thing it sometimes did around honest answers—annoyance and relief braided together so tightly they became one face.

"Good," she said quietly. "That will hold." 

She pushed off the frame, then paused in the doorway.

"Marcus."

"Yeah?"

"If you wake from protocol dreams, don't run. Call for me."

"Protocol dreams are a thing?"

"You're an Earth engineer with live guest-pass auth and fresh KO contact. They're definitely a thing."

"Great. Love this."

"Sleep anyway."

She left. The ward lights dimmed in sequence until his alcove sat in warm half-shadow.

Marcus lay back and listened to the hum.

Point-three seconds. Pulse.

Point-three seconds. Pulse.

Point-three seconds. Pulse.

He counted to fifty before sleep took him.

In the dream, he was back at his terminal in San Francisco.

The same mug. The same cold coffee ring. The same green cursor waiting on black.

Except now the log file was bilingual.

On the left side: familiar auth tokens, timestamps, routing records.

On the right side: tongue glyphs, six-phase signatures, DR checksums, RU verdicts.

One line pulsed brighter than the rest.

UNAUTHORIZED CHANNEL: HANDSHAKE ATTEMPT ACCEPTED.

SOURCE: EARTH-NATIVE NODE.

COUNTERPART: AETHERMOOR DEEP LAYER.

Marcus leaned toward the screen and saw something underneath the line, barely visible, like text printed on thermal paper before heat reveals it.

Not one voice.

Many.

A low, layered signal rising from somewhere below protocol level, below library stacks and training halls and carefully labeled tongue domains.

A swarm of requests speaking in near-unison, all carrying the same intent fingerprint, all trying to route upward through a channel that should not have existed.

When he woke, his wrist cuff was warm and his heart was sprinting.

The DR sigil above his bed was glowing red.

And from somewhere deep under the ward floor, he could feel—not hear, but feel—a synchronized pattern of knocks, precise as clockwork.

Three short.

Three long.

Three short.

A distress signal in a language made of timing.

Marcus sat up, breathing hard, and said the only thing his exhausted engineer brain could produce at four in the morning in a reality that had already replaced impossible with Tuesday.

"Cool," he whispered to no one. "So now the basement is talking."




Chapter 4: The Swarm Beneath

By the time Polly reached his alcove, Marcus had already put on one boot, forgotten the other, and attempted to stand up into a hanging lamp.

"Ow," he said, with the quiet dignity of a man losing a fight against architecture at dawn.

Polly, in raven form, looked at him from the doorway and made the exact same expression she used when junior Keepers submitted malformed declarations.

"You have blood on your forehead," she said.

"It's decorative."

"It's stupid."

"Mutually compatible design philosophies."

She hopped inside, monocle glinting in the ward's low light. "Status."
