"It's not wrong," she said.

Marcus looked at her.

"The system doesn't trust itself. That's by design. My father didn't build the six tongues because he trusted the world. He built them because he'd learned what trust costs when it's misplaced." She turned the cuff at her wrist — the small, habitual motion that Marcus had come to associate with Senna reaching for something that lay below her public composure. "The checking isn't weakness. It's grief made structural. The grief of someone who knew that even good systems go wrong if nobody watches."

The aurora started. Slow green bands threading through the upper atmosphere, reflected in the Spire's crystal skin until the whole tower looked like it was dreaming in color.

"You know what worries me more than the Void Seed?" Marcus said.

"Tell me."

"The day nobody comes down those stairs to argue with it. The day we decide it's handled. That's when it wins — not by convincing us, but by boring us into looking away."

Senna's expression did something subtle. The professional mask thinned by a fraction, and underneath it Marcus caught a glimpse of something that looked like relief. The relief of someone who had been carrying a fear alone and had just heard someone else name it.

"That," she said, "is why you're Third Circle."

They stood at the railing for a while after that, watching the pulse cross the city. Not speaking. Not needing to. By the time the aurora faded and the night settled into its deeper registers, neither of them was leaving enough space for a third person to walk between them.

* * *

Three weeks later, the alarm came during a training session.

Not a Void Seed alarm — those had a particular frequency, low and grinding, that Marcus had learned to distinguish from every other emergency signal. This was a perimeter alert. The Harmonic Wall's outer boundary, four clicks east of the governed zone, where the Protocol's verification coverage thinned to near-nothing and things from outside occasionally tested the edges.

Marcus dismissed the class. Yoon-Ji didn't leave.

"I want to see," she said.

"You're fourth-week. You don't have clearance."

"I didn't ask for clearance. I said I want to see."

Marcus remembered being fourth-week. He remembered Polly's expression when he'd said something similar. He remembered that she had let him see, and that the seeing had changed him in ways that mattered.

"Stay behind me," he said. "Don't touch anything. Don't speak to anything. If I say run, you run."

They reached the monitoring station in three minutes. Bram was already there, forearms tense, pulling up the perimeter display. Senna arrived thirty seconds later, Phasewing materializing at her feet before the rest of her had fully stopped moving.

The display showed the eastern boundary. A shape was pressing against the Wall — not physically, but dimensionally. It left impressions in the verification fabric the way a hand leaves impressions in wet clay. Not an incursion. Not a Rogue signature. Something else.

"Probe," Senna said. "Dominion-class. Testing response time."

"Kael?" Marcus asked.

"Not directly. His aesthetic. His patience." She pulled up the analysis overlay. "It's mapping our verification density. Counting how long it takes us to notice and how many resources we commit to a response."

"So it's intelligence gathering."

"It's homework," Senna said, with the dry precision of someone who had seen this pattern before and knew what came after homework. "The next incursion won't be improvised."

The probe withdrew after eleven minutes. It left no damage, no trace, no evidence except the dimensional impressions in the verification fabric — and those would fade within hours, which was, Marcus realized, exactly the point. If they hadn't been watching in real time, they would never have known it happened.

"How often?" Marcus asked.

"Monthly," Senna said. "For the past four months. This is the first one you've been here for."

Marcus looked at her. "You didn't tell me."

"I'm telling you now." Her eyes held something complicated — the calculus of someone who had been weighing when to share a burden against the cost of carrying it alone. "The probes are increasing in sophistication. The last three mapped different verification sectors. If you overlay them—"

"You get a complete survey of the Wall's response patterns," Marcus finished. "He's building a model of our defenses."

"Yes."

The room was very quiet. Behind them, Yoon-Ji stood motionless, her eyes moving between the display and the two Keepers with the focused attention of someone who had just understood, viscerally, that the system she was learning to defend was being studied by something that intended to break it.

"I want to help," Yoon-Ji said.

Marcus turned. He saw the fear in her face and, underneath the fear, the particular stubbornness of someone who had decided that fear was a data point and not a directive.

"Then learn faster," he said.

* * *

Senna found him on the eastern ring again that night. Neither of them mentioned the probe. They didn't need to. The knowledge sat between them like a third person — present, acknowledged, not requiring conversation.

What Senna said instead was: "New intake tomorrow. Three refugees. One of them is young."

"How young?"

"Twenty-three. Security researcher. Google. Name's Aisha Okonkwo."

Marcus felt the symmetry and immediately distrusted it. Young. Engineer. Google. The Protocol fishing in the same pond it had fished him from. He wondered if the universe was repeating a pattern or if he was seeing patterns because that was what his brain did when reality got uncomfortable.

"You're making a face," Senna said.

"I'm making my regular face."

"Your regular face doesn't furrow. This face furrows."

"I'm thinking about whether the Protocol selects for temperament or just for skill set."

"Both," Senna said, with the matter-of-fact certainty of someone who had watched twelve hundred refugees arrive over forty years. "It finds people who think in systems. But it also finds people who care about what systems protect. The skill gets them here. The caring keeps them."

Marcus looked at her. She looked back. Phasewing curled at her feet, briefly transparent, then solid again, as if privacy were a state it could modulate.

"Is that what kept you?" Marcus asked.

Senna's thumb found the groove in the stone. One trace. No more.

"What kept me," she said, "was that someone had to. And I was the one who was left."

The words landed with the weight of forty years of carrying a dead father's architecture and a living brother's betrayal. Marcus didn't reach for her hand. He didn't say anything comforting. He stood shoulder to shoulder with her at the railing and let the silence hold the weight, because some things broke if you tried to make them lighter.

After a while, she turned the cuff at her wrist and said, "You should be the one to greet her."

"The new intake?"

"Aisha. You remember what it's like. The disorientation. The anger. The part where you try to apply logic from one reality to another and the logic refuses to cooperate."

"And you think she needs someone who's been through it."

"I think she needs someone who's been through it and came out the other side still asking questions instead of just giving answers."

Marcus nodded. The aurora was starting again — green this time, with threads of gold that made the sky look like it was being embroidered by something patient and enormous.

"I'll be there," he said.

* * *

The intake channel flickered at nine the next morning. Crystal light scattered into motes. Three figures materialized in the transit circle, stumbling with the particular disorientation of people whose entire framework for understanding reality had just been invalidated.

Two men and a woman.

One of the men — tall, South Asian, still clutching a laptop bag that had somehow survived dimensional transit — immediately tried to open the laptop, presumably to run a diagnostic. The laptop was dead. He stared at it with the offended expression of someone whose primary coping mechanism had just been confiscated by physics.

The second man — Dax, Marcus realized, from the intake dossier: compact, weathered, with the thousand-yard stare of someone who'd been debugging production incidents at three in the morning for too many years — said nothing. He looked around the greeting hall with the systematic attention of someone cataloguing exits. Then he looked at Marcus. Then he looked at the exits again. Marcus appreciated the honesty.

The woman locked eyes with Marcus.

Aisha Okonkwo was twenty-three, dark-skinned, with close-cropped hair and engineer's calluses on her fingertips. She wore a Google hoodie that had seen better days. But where Marcus had expected to see his own reflection — the bewildered engineer, the angry skeptic — he saw something different.

She wasn't angry. She was *calculating.*

Her eyes moved across the greeting hall in a systematic sweep: architecture first, then exits, then people, then back to the architecture with a tighter focus. Not panic. Threat assessment. She was evaluating the room the way a security researcher evaluates an unfamiliar network — assuming hostility until proven otherwise, building a mental map of every possible vector.

"Where am I?" she said. Not demanded. Said. Flat, controlled, the tone of someone conducting an interview rather than experiencing a crisis. "What's the authentication layer on the light? And why does my phone have a pulse?"

Marcus blinked. He'd prepared for the "magic isn't real" argument. He had not prepared for someone who skipped the ontological crisis entirely and went straight to infrastructure questions.

"You noticed the authentication layer," he said.

"The light changes intensity on a regular interval. Point-three seconds. That's a heartbeat signal. Heartbeat signals mean something's being monitored. I want to know what." She tucked her dead phone into her pocket with the deliberate calm of someone shelving a tool that was no longer relevant. "Also, I'd like to know who you are, who's in charge, and whether I'm a prisoner or a guest, in that order."

"Guest," Marcus said. "I'm Keeper Chen. The person in charge is a committee, which I realize is not reassuring. And the light is Protocol verification — the system checking its own integrity sixty times per second."

Aisha's expression didn't change, but something behind her eyes sharpened. "Continuous self-verification. That implies the system expects to be compromised."

"The system *has* been compromised. We're in an ongoing defensive posture against a centralized threat actor who's been probing our perimeter for the past four months." He watched her reaction. No flinch. No widening eyes. Just a small nod, as if he'd confirmed something she'd already suspected. "You're not surprised."

"I'm a security researcher. Any system worth attacking is worth defending. And any system that spends this much energy on verification has enemies worth worrying about." She looked past him, toward the orientation chamber. "What's behind that door?"

"Six pedestals. Six spheres. The foundational architecture of the security framework you just noticed in the light." He gestured. "I can explain, or I can show you. The second option is faster."

"Show me."

He led them toward the orientation chamber — all three, though Aisha was the one asking questions and the other two were still in the processing-reality phase that Marcus remembered like a bruise. At the door, he paused.

"One thing," he said. "You're going to want to call it code. Infrastructure. Protocol architecture. All of those words are accurate. But they're translations. The native term is magic, and the native population uses it without irony, and if you spend your first two weeks correcting them, you'll miss the part where they're right."

Aisha's eyes narrowed. "Magic is a word for systems people haven't reverse-engineered yet."

"That's what I said when I got here. I was wrong. Not about the engineering. About the 'yet.' Some of this doesn't reduce. Some of it is genuinely irreducible to mechanism. The system works, the math is sound, and the explanation stops at a layer that doesn't have a bottom." He opened the door. "That used to bother me. Now it's the most interesting thing about the job."

Inside: six pedestals, six spheres, six colors. KO red-gold. AV blue-silver. RU violet. CA white-gold. UM shadow-black. DR amber-earth.

The Protocol pulsed. Point-three seconds. Check. Point-three seconds. Check.

"Welcome to the Archives," Marcus said. "Let me tell you about the Protocol."

Aisha stepped inside. She didn't look afraid. She didn't look awed. She looked like someone walking into a server room for the first time — aware that every blinking light meant something, determined to learn what.

Behind Marcus, standing in the doorway, Polly watched.

She was in raven form. Graduation cap slightly askew. Monocle catching the tongue-light. Obsidian eyes bright with something that was not quite pride and not quite grief and not quite hope but the thing that three hundred years of carrying a chain produces when the chain grows a new link.

*He's good at this,* she thought.

Not the teaching. Not the diplomacy. Not the seven years of service and the battles and the proof and the thousand small acts of competence that had made him Third Circle.

The thing Marcus was good at — the thing Polly had recognized the moment a confused engineer from San Francisco had argued with her about the word "magic" — was the same thing Izack had been good at. The same thing the first Keepers had been good at when they shattered themselves into Echoes to save a world from a god.

He was good at choosing to stay.

Not because it was easy. Not because it was safe. Not because some grand destiny had selected him. But because he'd seen the system, understood the system, found the flaws in the system, and decided that the flaws were features and the system was worth defending.

That was the whole thing.

That was the only thing that mattered.

Polly ruffled her feathers, adjusted her cap, and settled onto the doorframe to listen to Marcus teach the next generation the six words that made the world work.

Behind her, deep below the Spire, below the stairs and the stone and the inscriptions —

The Void Seed pulsed.

Patient. Persistent. Correct about everything except the one thing that mattered.

And the chains held.

Not because they were strong.

Because they were six.

And somewhere outside the governed zone, in the dimensional spaces where the Harmonic Wall's light faded to uncertainty, a probe that had not been improvised was already returning with new data. The next incursion was being designed. Not by the Void Seed alone. By someone who had once held all six tongues and remembered how they fit together.

The long watch was not over. The long watch was the point.

And one floor above, in a room full of crystal light and bad coffee, someone new was learning how to belong — not by believing in magic, but by refusing to stop asking how it worked.



Chapter 18: The Fizzlecress Incident

The trouble started, as most trouble in Aethermoor started, with something Bram would later describe as "a series of individually defensible decisions."

Marcus was sitting on the roof of the eastern annex at eleven o'clock at night, holding a mug of bloom liquor that Dax had produced from a coat pocket with the practiced discretion of a man who had been smuggling beverages past institutional oversight since his undergraduate days at Melbourne.

"Third cup," Dax said.

"I'm aware."

"You're crying."

"I am not crying. I'm experiencing an involuntary ocular response to—"

"You're crying, mate." Dax refilled the mug with the generous precision of an Australian who had decided that someone's emotional infrastructure needed emergency maintenance. "You've been crying since you started talking about kimchi."

Marcus wiped his face with the back of his hand. The bloom liquor was warm and sweet and made his teeth hum and was absolutely, categorically not the reason his eyes were leaking. The reason his eyes were leaking was that he'd been thinking about his mother's kimchi jjigae — the specific kind she made on Sundays, the kind where the kimchi was old enough to be sour and the pork was cheap and the broth was the color of sunset and the whole apartment smelled like garlic and gochugaru and home, *home*, the word he hadn't said aloud in three years because saying it broke something he needed to keep functional.

The thing people got wrong about being taken was they imagined the last moment. Cold coffee, green terminal, line 4,847. But that wasn't the memory that cut. The memory that cut was the Thursday before — two days earlier, a lifetime ago. He'd gone to Danny's place after work. Danny and Priya and that kid from the platform team whose name Marcus could never remember. They'd ordered Thai. They'd watched something terrible on Netflix and argued about whether it was terrible on purpose. Priya had fallen asleep on the couch. Marcus had thrown a blanket over her and Danny had said "you're going to be a good dad someday" and Marcus had said "I'm going to be a terrible dad who is excellent at incident response" and they'd laughed and he'd walked home at midnight through the fog and the city had smelled like eucalyptus and exhaust and he had hated his job and loved his friends and thought, *this is enough. This specific life, with these specific people, in this specific city, is enough.*

Two days later the screen went white and he never went back.

"I miss my air conditioner," Marcus said.

"Right."

"I miss having an air conditioner in a room where the temperature is a number that I control with a button, and the number stays where I put it, and nobody asks me to declare my intent before the room gets cold."

"That's reasonable."

"I miss television. I miss the specific experience of sitting on a couch that is ugly and comfortable and watching something that does not matter. Something with a laugh track. Something where nobody is verified every point-three seconds and the stakes are whether the funny man gets the girl and the answer is always yes."

Dax nodded with the solemn attention of a man who had processed this exact grief and come out the other side with functional scar tissue. He had a tattoo of a kangaroo on his left forearm that he'd gotten at nineteen and regretted at twenty and defended at thirty because at some point a bad tattoo becomes an honest one. He'd been debugging Kubernetes clusters at three in the morning when the Protocol took him. But like Marcus, his real last memory wasn't the work. It was earlier that day — a barbecue in his mate's backyard, cold beer, somebody's dog stealing a sausage off the grill. The ordinary stuff. The stuff that only becomes sacred after you can't have it anymore.

"I miss my computer," Marcus said. "My actual computer. The one I built with my own hands from parts I chose because I am insane and particular. It had three monitors. Not crystal. Not alive. Not verified. Just screens that showed me what I asked to see and did not have opinions about my emotional coherence."

"I miss my friends," he added, quieter. "Danny and Priya. I miss being in a room where nobody is trying to save the world and we're just watching something bad on TV and arguing about whether it's bad on purpose. I miss being bored. Being bored is a privilege and I didn't know it."

"I had a mechanical keyboard," Dax said. "Cherry MX Blues. The loud ones."

"The *loud* ones."

"My flatmate hated me."

"Everybody's flatmate hated them. That's what flatmates are for."

They drank. The aurora was doing its thing overhead — violet and gold, slow-moving, beautiful in a way that Marcus had learned to find normal and that still, on nights like this, ambushed him with the knowledge that he would never again see a sky that was just a sky.

"You're not actually homesick," Dax said after a while.

"I'm clearly homesick."

"Nah. I was homesick my first year. Homesick is wanting to go back. You don't want to go back. You want to want to go back, because wanting to go back means you haven't given up on the place you came from, and giving up on the place you came from feels like betrayal." Dax took a sip. "What's actually wrong is you're in love with Senna Thorne and it's scaring the absolute piss out of you."

Marcus's mug stopped halfway to his mouth.

"That is—"

"Completely, painfully, architecturally obvious to every person in this Spire who has functioning eyes." Dax leaned back against the parapet. "Bram told me. Bram. The man who once failed to notice a structural fire because it was on the wrong side of a load-bearing wall and therefore 'not his department.' Even Bram can see it."

Marcus said nothing.

"You look for her in every room," Dax continued, gentler now. "When she's not there, your shoulders drop. When she is there, your voice goes half a register lower and you start doing the thing where you explain something technical and then check whether she's following — not because you doubt her, but because you want her to see the thing you see. That's not professional collaboration, Chen. That's a man building a bridge and hoping the other person walks across it."

Marcus set the mug down.

"I can't."

"You can."

"She is the Chair of Operational Continuity. She holds the system together. Her father built the world and her brother almost broke it and she has spent forty years being the person who doesn't get to feel things because feeling things is a luxury the continuity function can't afford." Marcus's voice cracked. He let it. The bloom liquor had filed down his usual defenses to the point where the crack felt like a feature rather than a failure. "I'm not going to be the variable that destabilizes her architecture."

Dax was quiet for a moment.

"My wife," he said. "Back on Earth. We met at a hackathon. She was debugging a memory leak in a React app and I said 'that's a dangling reference' and she said 'so is your haircut' and I knew right then I was going to marry her or die trying." He rolled the mug between his palms. "I didn't get to say goodbye. The Protocol took me mid-sleep. I woke up in a crystal room with a raven in a graduation cap telling me my existence was temporary, and all I could think was that Sarah was going to wake up to an empty bed and think I'd left."

Marcus looked at him.

"She'll think I'm dead by now," Dax said. "Or missing. Or one of those unsolved cases they make podcasts about. And I can't send a message. I can't even dream about her properly because the Protocol reformatted my sleep architecture." He took a breath. "So when I tell you that you're wasting time being afraid of the one good thing this place has offered you, I'm saying it from the perspective of a man who would give both kidneys and his terrible kangaroo tattoo for one more morning with his wife."

The aurora pulsed. The bloom liquor hummed.

Marcus picked up his mug and drained it.

"I'm going to need another one of these," he said.

"That's the spirit. Figuratively and literally."

"How many do you have?"

Dax reached into the coat and produced a bottle with a label Marcus couldn't read. "Enough for bad decisions."

"I don't make bad decisions."

"You're crying on a roof about a woman you won't talk to. You are the mayor of Bad Decision City."

Marcus laughed. It came out wet and broken and honest and he let it.

"She touches her cuff," he said. "Her left hand. When she's thinking. When she's scared. When she's forcing herself to stay composed. It's the one thing she can't control. And every time I see her do it, I want to—"

He stopped.

"Put your hand over hers," Dax finished.

"Yeah."

"Then do it, you absolute drongo."

They drank until the bottle was empty and the aurora was doing things that Marcus was fairly certain auroras were not supposed to do, and at some point Marcus started drawing heating curves on the parapet wall with a chalk nub from his pocket and explaining how you could reverse-engineer a proper roasting profile from crystal resonance frequencies and Polly's discarded monocle lenses and Dax said "that's either genius or arson" and Marcus said "on Earth we call that engineering" and Dax laughed so hard he spilled bloom liquor down his shirt, and then things went sideways in a manner that neither of them would fully reconstruct.

* * *

Marcus woke up to the smell of something that was almost coffee.

Almost. The way a cover band is almost the real song — the notes were there, the structure was there, the emotional intent was recognizable, but there was an additional harmonic that the original never had. A fizzing. A warmth that started in the back of his throat and spread outward like someone had wired his nervous system to a very gentle voltage source.

He opened his eyes.

He was not on the roof.

He was sitting at a table approximately eight inches tall, in a room approximately nine feet in diameter, inside what appeared to be a miniature tropical island contained within a glass sphere the size of a beach ball. Through the curved glass walls, he could see the interior of a workshop so chaotic it made his university dorm look like a clean room. Shelves of beakers, coils of copper wire, three active experiments that were either boiling or plotting escape, and a chalkboard covered in equations that Marcus's detection faculty registered as both mathematically sound and existentially threatening.

The table he was sitting at was driftwood. The chair was a coconut shell, split and sanded. There was a tiny palm tree casting actual shade over his head. The sand beneath his bare feet was warm.

He was inside the globe.

On the other side of the table, also eight inches tall relative to the sphere's interior, sat the most disorganized genius Marcus had ever encountered.

Fizzle Brightcog was approximately three feet tall in real-world scale, which at their current miniaturization made him slightly shorter than Marcus in a way that felt democratically absurd. Half-gnome on his mother's side, half-dwarf on his father's — a combination that, as Fizzle had apparently explained to Marcus at length during the Coff-three phase, meant he'd inherited gnomish innovation and dwarven stubbornness in roughly equal measure and they had been arguing inside him for his entire life. The gnome half wanted to build things that didn't exist yet. The dwarf half wanted to build things that would last forever. The compromise was inventions that were simultaneously brilliant and on fire.

He had the broad hands of a Deepstone craftsman — thick-fingered, scarred from forge work, capable of precision that belied their size — but the restless, darting eyes of a gnomish tinkerer who had already moved on to the next idea before the current one finished exploding. His hair was white-grey and pointed in seven directions, each one apparently independent of the others, as if his scalp had opinions about aerodynamics and couldn't reach consensus. He wore goggles pushed up on his forehead, a leather apron singed in eleven distinct locations, and an expression of absolute delight that had not dimmed, Marcus suspected, since approximately the Founding.

He spoke in Draumric when he was concentrating — the forge tongue, his father's language, the one that felt like stone and tasted like hot iron — and switched to something faster and more melodic when excited, a gnomish dialect Marcus's translation layer rendered as a stream of ideas with occasional punctuation.

"You're awake!" he said. "Wonderful! How's the coffee?"

Marcus looked down. There was a cup in his hand. He had apparently been drinking it in his sleep. The cup was warm, ceramic, and hand-thrown with the specific lopsided charm of someone who had made a cup once and decided they'd solved pottery.

He took a sip.

It was good.

Not good the way Bram's coffee was good, which was not at all. Not good the way Earth coffee was good, which was a memory that ached. Good in a way that was entirely its own thing — rich, dark, bitter with a finish of something like roasted chestnuts and burnt caramel, and underneath that the fizzing warmth that was spreading through his body like a controlled chain reaction.

"This is actual coffee," Marcus said.

"It is not," Fizzle said happily. "It is *better*. I reverse-engineered it from the metabolic trace you left on Bram's mug last year."

"You analyzed my saliva."

"Saliva, cortisol profile, dopamine-response curve, and the specific frequency your brain emits when it's receiving caffeine. Then I cross-referenced that with seven local alkaloid sources, two root extracts, and a mineral deposit from under the Harmonic Wall that is technically classified." He beamed. "I call it Coff-three."

"Coff-three."

"Coff-one caught fire. Coff-two achieved sentience briefly and had to be talked down from the shelf. Coff-three is stable." He paused. "Mostly."

Marcus took another sip. The fizzing intensified pleasantly. His brain felt like it was being cleaned with warm light.

"Why am I four inches tall and sitting inside a glass island?"

"My Workshop Globe!" Fizzle gestured at the curved walls with the pride of someone showing off a yacht. "Pocket-dimension prototype. Izack helped me calibrate the spatial compression. The island is modeled after a place in one of those Earth picture-box stories he used to describe — a small house on a small island where a wise old man lives alone and trains young people."

"Master Roshi," Marcus said.

"Yes! The turtle man!" He said it the way people say things about friends they haven't seen in too long — present tense, careful enthusiasm, as if speaking in the past would make the distance real. "Izack was very fond of the turtle man. He said any wise person who chooses to live on an island alone is either enlightened or avoiding something, and the best ones are both." Fizzle poured himself more Coff-three from a pot that was boiling over a flame source Marcus couldn't identify. "I added the palm tree. Izack said the original had one. I felt it was essential for atmospheric integrity."

Marcus looked at the palm tree. It was swaying in a breeze that couldn't exist inside a glass sphere but apparently did.

"How did I get in here?"

"You climbed in! At approximately two in the morning. You and the Melbourne man. You were both extremely energized from the Coff-three I gave you at the pub and you said, and I am quoting from memory, 'Fizzle, I need to be somewhere small where nobody can find me because I have too many feelings and they are all load-bearing.'"

"That sounds like something I would say."

"The Melbourne man said, 'Same but with fewer syllables.' He left around four. You stayed." He poured himself more Coff-three and settled into his coconut chair with the air of someone about to enjoy himself. "Before the equations, we talked. You were very forthcoming."

Marcus's stomach dropped. "Forthcoming about what?"

"Everything! Your mother. Your father — absent, correct? You said 'he left when I was six and the most useful thing he taught me was that systems fail.' Then you cried a little. Then you talked about your grandmother's kitchen. Yellow curtains. Sesame oil. You were very specific about the curtains."

"I don't—"

"Then you talked about the woman with the cuff." Fizzle's eyes sparkled with the particular delight of someone who had been alive since before most mountains and still found human romance endlessly entertaining. He leaned forward on the driftwood table. "You talked about her for quite a long time, Marcus. You described her thumb tracing a groove in stone. You described the way her composure breaks — not into vulnerability but into the person underneath. You used the phrase 'structurally elegant' about her jawline, which I noted as a unique approach to courtship."

"Please stop."

"You also asked me if I'd ever been in love, which I thought was charming. I told you about Izack and the time he accidentally proposed to a dimensional constant instead of to Aria because he was doing math and talking at the same time. The constant said yes. Aria found it very funny for about three hundred years."

"Did I ask about your family?"

"You did! You asked whether I had children. I told you I had seven hundred and forty-two experiments, most of which I loved, some of which had tried to kill me, and one of which—" he gestured at the chicken in safety goggles "—had simply refused to be anything other than what it was. That seemed to move you. You said 'that's parenting' and then you drew the brewing diagrams." He pointed at the driftwood table.

Marcus looked down.

The table was covered in chalk equations. His handwriting, interspersed with Fizzle's — his precise and angular, Fizzle's explosive and annotated with tiny drawings of beakers, explosions, and what appeared to be a chicken wearing safety goggles.

He read the equations.

Then he read them again.

"Fizzle."

"Mm?"

"Did we... build a phase-modulated brewing cycle that uses the Protocol's verification pulse as a temperature regulator?"

Fizzle squinted at the table. "It appears so, yes."

"That would mean the coffee brews differently every point-three seconds, adjusting extraction rate to the drinker's current metabolic state."

"Adaptive coffee," Fizzle said. "Personalized to the specific needs of the specific person at the specific moment."

Marcus stared at the equations. They were beautiful. They were insane. They were also, as far as he could tell, correct.

"We invented living coffee."

"You invented living coffee. I provided the infrastructure and the island." Fizzle took a triumphant sip from his own cup. "Also, you should know that Coff-three has a side effect."

"What side effect?"

"The alkaloid profile induces approximately four hours of heightened cognitive activity followed by—" he made a gentle descending whistle with his mouth "— quite sudden and comprehensive inebriation. The wiring phase, I believe you called it. And then the unwiring."

Marcus felt the blood leave his face. "The drinking on the roof."

"Was the unwiring phase, yes. You and the Melbourne man consumed Coff-three at my workshop at approximately six in the evening. By ten you were, and I believe this is his phrase, 'wired to the tits.' By eleven you were on the roof with bloom liquor accelerating the unwiring. By two you were in my globe drawing phase diagrams and crying about a woman with a silver cuff."

"I did not—"

"You drew a picture of the cuff." Fizzle pointed at the corner of the table.

Marcus looked.

It was there. A small, precise sketch of Senna's wrist. The cuff. The groove her thumb traced. Drawn with the hyper-focused accuracy of a man who had been staring at that cuff for three years and knew its topography better than his own apartment in San Francisco.

*Former apartment. Not home. Former apartment.*

"I made adaptive coffee and drew pictures of a woman's wrist while four inches tall on a fake island inside a glass ball," Marcus said.

"Yes."

"This is what my life is now."

"Yes! Isn't it wonderful?"

Marcus looked at Fizzle Brightcog — four inches of chaotic genius, Izack's oldest friend, a man who had built a pocket dimension modeled after an anime character's island and saw no contradiction between classified mineral deposits and hand-thrown pottery — and felt something he hadn't expected.

Not embarrassment. Not regret. Not the cold morning-after clarity that usually followed bad decisions.

Gratitude.

"Fizzle," he said.

"Yes?"

"The coffee is really good."

He lit up like a six-tongue verification cascade completing consensus.

"I know," he said. "Coff-four will be even better. I'm adding a memory-stabilization component so the unwiring phase doesn't produce emotional honesty."

"Don't."

Fizzle tilted his head.

"Don't remove the emotional honesty part," Marcus said. He looked at the sketch of Senna's cuff. "I think I needed it."

Fizzle studied him for a long moment with eyes that were, underneath the chaos and the goggles and the singed apron, as old and as kind as the man who had been his friend since before the Founding.

"Izack used to say," he said, "that the best tools are the ones that show you what your hands were already doing." He scratched the chicken behind its goggles. "I think he meant people, not tools. But Izack was always better at saying things about objects than about feelings."

Marcus finished the coffee.

The globe was warm. The palm tree swayed. Somewhere outside the glass, in the real-size world, the Spire was waking up, and Bram's coffee machine was losing another argument with thermodynamics, and Senna Thorne was already awake, touching her cuff in the pre-dawn dark, reviewing continuity logs for a system that held fifty thousand people together and did not know that one of those people had spent the night drawing pictures of her wrist on a driftwood table inside a mad scientist's snow globe.

"How do I get out of the island?" Marcus asked.

"Oh! That part is easy. You simply declare your intent to leave and the spatial compression reverses."

"Just like that?"

"Just like that. Though I should mention — the expansion is rapid. You will be full-sized quite suddenly. I recommend not being near the shelf with the volatile compounds."

Marcus stood up from the coconut chair, brushed sand off his pants that would not exist at full scale, and looked at the tiny island one more time.

"Fizzle."

"Yes, dear?"

"I'm going to come back. Not for the coffee. For the island. I need a place where the problems are small."

"The problems are the same size," Fizzle said. "You're just closer to them. But I understand the sentiment. The door is always open." He paused. "Metaphorically. The actual door has a seventeen-tongue lock that I invented while bored and cannot fully reverse. Knock three times and whistle in Draumric."

Marcus laughed.

Then he declared his intent to leave, and the world rushed back to its proper size, and he was standing in Fizzle Brightcog's Experimental Chaos Workshop at seven in the morning with chalk on his hands and a sketch of a silver cuff in his pocket and the taste of the best coffee he'd had since Earth still fizzing on his tongue.

Dax was asleep on a workbench, the kangaroo tattoo visible on his forearm. He was snoring in a rhythm that was, Marcus noticed, exactly synchronized with the Protocol's heartbeat.

Marcus left him there. Fizzle would take care of it. Fizzle took care of everything in his orbit, the way stars took care of gravity: not intentionally, but as a consequence of mass.

He walked back toward the Spire in the early light. The aurora had faded. The morning was crystal-clear and smelled like dew on stone.

He needed a shower. He needed real food. He needed to check his lab notes and verify that the sign error Polly had caught yesterday was actually fixed and not just moved.

But first.

First he was going to find Senna on the eastern ring.

And he was going to stand beside her, shoulder to shoulder, the way he always did.

And at some point — not today, maybe not this week, but soon, because Dax was right and the time you spent being afraid of the one good thing was time you didn't get back —

He was going to put his hand over hers when she reached for the cuff.

And see what happened next.

* * *

Three floors below, in the Experimental Chaos Workshop, Fizzle Brightcog regarded the equations on his globe-table with the careful attention of someone who had been doing science since before most of the world's current mountains had finished growing.

He copied the phase-modulated brewing equations onto proper paper.

Filed them under "Coff-three: Adaptive Protocol, v. Chen-Brightcog."

And started work on Coff-four.

The chicken in the safety goggles watched from the shelf. It had opinions. Fizzle respected them.

Science, after all, was collaborative.



Chapter 19: The Outer Ring

The Council did not ask.

Third Circle operators received assignments, not invitations. The distinction mattered because invitations implied the option to decline, and what the Council needed done at the edges of Avalon was not the kind of work that benefited from optional participation.

"Field audit," Tovak said, handing Marcus a crystal slate with six seal impressions already locked into its surface. "Every perimeter node. Every relay. Every boundary where the tongues touch open dimensional space. You verify that the Six Tongues are holding at the edges, not just the core."

Marcus looked at the slate. The seals were Council-grade: Senna's opaline CA signature, Tovak's amber-black UM mark, four others he recognized from governance sessions. Full operational authority. The kind of document that said *we trust you to find bad news and not flinch.*

"The First Incursion showed us something," Tovak continued. "Kael's copies aren't just probing the center. Three of them watched and took notes. The next attack won't come from the vault. It will come from wherever we're thinnest."

"And you think we're thin."

"I think we haven't looked in nine years." Tovak's expression carried the particular weight of someone who had maintained infrastructure long enough to know that the scariest failures were the ones nobody had checked recently. "You built the fractal detection model. You understand what you're looking for. Go find out what the edges look like."

Marcus studied the route map embedded in the slate. Seven destinations. Seven different expressions of the same system. Starting with the closest: the Floating Shelves of the Eastern Rise, where Alexander Thorne ran perimeter defense for Avalon's outermost boundary.

"Alexander Thorne," Marcus said.

"Yes."

"The eldest."

"The eldest," Tovak confirmed. "Who has not visited the Spire in fourteen years and who communicates with the Council through quarterly status reports that are exactly as long as they need to be and not one word longer."

"You're sending me to audit the man who was running perimeter defense before I was born on Earth."

"I'm sending you to audit the system he defends. Whether Alexander cooperates is between you and Alexander."

Marcus looked at Polly, who was perched on the console behind Tovak with her graduation cap at an angle that suggested she had opinions about this assignment but was choosing not to share them until the timing would cause maximum educational impact.

"You're coming," Marcus said.

"Obviously." Her feathers ruffled once, settled. "Someone has to translate between an engineer and a soldier."

* * *

The Floating Shelves were not what Marcus expected.

He had expected impressive. The geography reference said "chain of hovering plateaus along ley currents" and "entire mountain peaks levitate and drift," which painted a picture of grand, serene, elevated surfaces drifting through clean sky like continental aircraft carriers.

What he got was a military installation that happened to float.

The transit circle deposited him on the largest shelf — a plateau roughly the size of a city block, hovering three hundred meters above Avalon's eastern forest canopy. The surface was scored with landing marks, equipment scars, and the kind of organized mess that accumulates when practical people have been working in the same space for decades without anyone from headquarters visiting to suggest they tidy up.

Wind hit him immediately. Not gentle atmospheric movement — the sustained, bone-deep push of air flowing around a floating object at altitude, carrying with it the taste of ozone and raw ley energy and something else Marcus couldn't name. Something thinner than the Spire. A frequency he was used to hearing at full strength now operating at maybe sixty percent, stretched across too much open space.

The tongues were thinner here. He felt it before he measured it.

At the Spire, the Six Sacred Tongues sang through crystal infrastructure with a density that made the air feel structured, every breath carrying verification data so thick you could almost taste the consensus. Here, at the edge, the song was still present but attenuated — like listening to a symphony through a wall. You could hear it. You could follow it. But the wall was there, and pretending otherwise was the kind of denial that got perimeter stations killed.

"Keeper Chen."

The voice came from behind a bank of equipment that Marcus's systems-trained eye identified as relay infrastructure: crystal arrays mounted on mechanical gimbals, each one oriented toward a different point in the surrounding sky, each one pulsing with tongue-light that looked more like signal flares than the ambient glow of the Spire.

Alexander Rune Thorne stepped into view.

Marcus had seen his portrait in the gallery — the confident eldest with one hand on Aethis's head, radiating responsibility like it was a form of body heat. The portrait had been accurate the way a passport photo is accurate: technically correct, emotionally misleading.

The real Alexander Thorne was taller than his portrait suggested. Broader. His dark hair was cut short in a way that said *I don't have time for this to be a thing* and his eyes — Izack's eyes, unmistakably, though harder at the edges — conducted a threat assessment of Marcus in the time it took most people to say hello.

He wore a utility coat similar to the one Grey had worn in his portrait, covered in tool loops and crystal mounts and the kind of repairs that said the coat had been functional for longer than most of Marcus's professional career. On his shoulder sat a creature that looked like a small golden hawk with scales instead of feathers and eyes that contained the particular intelligence of something that was very aware it was bonded to a Sacred Egg.

He also looked tired in the way only durable people did: not near collapse, not even visibly strained, just permanently in debt to rest and too disciplined to mention it.

Aethis. The amber companion. Heart-bonded since childhood.

A few yards behind him stood a dark mare in perimeter tack, one forehoof cocked, head lowered into the wind as if she were listening through it instead of merely enduring it. Static runes moved through her mane in quiet blue-white crawls, brightening whenever the thinner tongue-fields hit a rough patch and subsiding when the local geometry settled. She was larger than any horse Marcus had known on Earth and calmer than most people he worked with. One amber-brown eye tracked him with the patient emotional accuracy of something that had already decided he was overtired and not yet dangerous.

Stormwind.

Navigator of paradox storms, Marcus remembered from the portrait wall. Emotional mount. Runic hoofprints detect traps.

"Tovak sent you," Alexander said. Not a question. His voice was low, precise, and carried the cadence of someone who rationed words the way a quartermaster rationed supplies.

"The Council sent me. Tovak delivered the paperwork."

The faintest shift in Alexander's expression. Not quite approval, but an acknowledgment that Marcus had identified the correct chain of authority. Military minds noticed those things.

"Your audit scope?"

Marcus held up the crystal slate. "Every boundary where the tongues touch open dimensional space. Verification that the Six Tongues protocol is holding integrity at the perimeter."

Alexander looked at the slate. Looked at the seals. Looked at Marcus. Then he turned and walked toward the edge of the plateau without checking whether Marcus was following.

Marcus followed.

Polly, from his shoulder, murmured: "That went well."

"That went neutral."

"For Alexander, neutral *is* well."

* * *

The perimeter defense network was extraordinary.

Not beautiful. Not elegant. Extraordinary in the way that a bridge built by someone who understood load-bearing was extraordinary: every element existed because it needed to, every connection served a purpose, and the whole thing worked not because it was inspired but because it was correct.

Alexander walked Marcus through the outer ring in three hours, speaking in a continuous technical briefing that never rose above conversational volume and never wasted a syllable.

"Twelve relay stations on the major shelves. Forty-eight sub-relays on the minor platforms. Each one maintains a six-tongue verification field that extends outward approximately two kilometers. The fields overlap by thirty percent to account for ley-current drift and atmospheric interference."

Marcus ran numbers in his head. "That's... nine hundred and sixty square kilometers of coverage with built-in redundancy."

"Correct."

"How many operators?"

"Twenty-three. Fourteen full-time, nine rotating."

Marcus stopped walking. "Twenty-three people covering nine hundred and sixty square kilometers of dimensional boundary?"

Alexander's expression didn't change, which Marcus was beginning to understand was itself communicative. In the language of Alexander Thorne, a non-reaction to a reasonable objection meant *yes, I know, and I've been solving this problem for longer than you've been asking about it.*

"The coverage is adequate," Alexander said. "The tongues do most of the work. We monitor, maintain, and respond to anomalies."

"Adequate." Marcus looked out over the edge of the shelf. Below, the eastern forest spread in every direction, canopy moving in the wind like a green ocean. Other shelves hung in the air at various altitudes, connected by ley-bridges that shimmered with AV blue-silver. It looked like someone had taken a mountain range, shattered it, and then suspended the pieces at convenient operating heights. "How often do you get anomalies?"

"Seventeen in the last year. Four genuine incursion attempts. Two signature corruptions. Eleven false positives caused by atmospheric ley-interference."

"Four genuine incursion attempts in one year."

"Down from nine the year before. The routing optimizations reduced false-negative rates and allowed faster response to genuine threats."

Marcus's internal alarm went off before the sentence finished. "What routing optimizations?"

Alexander stopped walking. He didn't turn around immediately. When he did, his expression had shifted — not dramatically, not emotionally, but into the particular stillness of someone who had been waiting for this question for a very long time.

"You already know," Alexander said.

Marcus's chest tightened. "Tell me anyway."

Alexander looked out at the relay network. Twelve stations blinking on the horizon, each one a node in a web of trust that kept Avalon's border intact.

"Forty percent of this network's routing logic was written by Kael Nightwhisper," Alexander said. "Specifically, the adaptive relay prioritization, the tongue-weight balancing algorithm, and the anomaly pattern-matching filter that reduced our false positive rate from sixty percent to eleven percent."

The wind pushed against Marcus's face. Polly's talons tightened on his shoulder.

"Forty percent," Marcus repeated.

"Kael was brilliant." Alexander said the word without admiration or bitterness, just the flat precision of an engineering assessment. "He understood distributed systems the way most people understand breathing. He wrote the routing optimizations when he was nineteen, before the Council knew what he was becoming. By the time they erased his name, his code was load-bearing."

"And you couldn't remove it."

"I could remove it. The network would lose sixty percent of its efficiency and we'd be blind to half the dimensional approaches for approximately four months while I rebuilt the logic from scratch." Alexander looked at Marcus with his father's eyes and his mother's precision. "During which time, four to nine incursion attempts would go undetected."

Marcus understood. He understood with the particular clarity of an engineer who had inherited production systems built by people who were no longer available to explain their design choices.

"You've been patching around his code."

"For thirty-one years. I have documented every modification, every workaround, every place where his logic interfaces with mine. The documentation fills eleven archive volumes." Alexander paused. "Jorren would approve of the documentation, if no one else does."

The joke was so dry it almost didn't register as a joke. Polly made a sound that might have been a laugh and might have been a sneeze and was probably both.

"Does the Council know?" Marcus asked.

"The Council knows that the perimeter defense network operates at acceptable efficiency. They receive quarterly reports confirming this. The reports are accurate."

"That's not what I asked."

Alexander met his eyes. "No. It isn't."

They stood at the edge of the floating shelf, wind pressing against them, the tongue-fields humming their attenuated song across the sky. Below, the forest breathed. Above, the other shelves drifted on their ley-current anchors, each one a small island of order floating in a very large sky.

"My father built this world," Alexander said, and it was the first time his voice had carried anything besides professional precision. Not emotion exactly. Weight. "My brother wrote the code that keeps its borders intact. Both of those things are true. I don't get to choose which truth is more convenient."

Marcus thought about the Void Seed. About the argument it made every month, patient and persistent and correct about everything except error correction.

"No," Marcus said. "You don't."

Alexander looked at him for a long moment. Then he nodded once — a small motion, barely visible, that contained more respect than most people communicated in paragraphs.

"Your audit will find the same pattern at every boundary you visit," Alexander said. "Kael didn't just optimize the center. He optimized everything he could reach. And he could reach further than any of us knew."

"How do you know what I'll find at the other sites?"

"Because I've been watching the edge for thirty-one years, and the edge is where you see the whole picture." Alexander turned back toward the relay stations. "The Spire thinks the system is the Spire. Out here, you learn that the system is the space between things. The connections. The gaps. The places where the signal gets thin and you have to decide whether to trust the protocol or trust yourself."

"What do you decide?"

Alexander looked back at Marcus. On his shoulder, Aethis ruffled golden-scaled wings and settled, a gesture so similar to Polly's that Marcus felt a brief, disorienting echo of recognition.

"Both," Alexander said. "That's the only answer that works."

* * *

Marcus stayed at the Floating Shelves for three days.

He audited every relay. Mapped every routing path. Documented every place where Kael's code interfaced with Alexander's patches, every seam where thirty-one years of workarounds had created a secondary architecture as complex and load-bearing as the original.

The picture was clear, and it was not comfortable.

The tongues at the edge were thinner. They worked, but they worked the way a bridge works at ninety percent capacity: reliably, until the day you needed that last ten percent and discovered it wasn't there. Alexander's operation was superb — disciplined, documented, maintained with the obsessive care of someone who understood that duty was not a feeling but a daily choice to keep showing up.

On the second night a paradox front scraped the eastern shelves hard enough to make the horizon duplicate itself.

Marcus heard the alarm tone before he understood what he was seeing: one relay tower in front of him, the same tower displaced twenty feet to the left, and a third version trying unsuccessfully to become both. Operators moved at once. Alexander was already in motion. Stormwind met him halfway, not skittish, not dramatic, simply present at the exact point the system had become most unstable. He mounted in one practiced movement and rode directly onto a ley-bridge that was trying to decide whether it still believed in direction.

Stormwind's docility did not vanish under stress. It refined. Her ears went forward. The static in her mane sharpened to a blue-white crackle. Each hoofprint flashed in clean rune geometry and held a stable coordinate just long enough for the relay crews to retune around it. She moved through the broken weather with the calm violence of something that had done this often enough to treat paradox as difficult terrain rather than apocalypse. Watching her, Marcus understood why Izack had trusted a living animal with storms that could make maps lie.

But it was held together by one man's decision to carry a burden he never talked about.

On the last evening, Marcus stood at the edge of the highest shelf and watched the sun set through nine hundred and sixty square kilometers of defended space. The tongue-fields caught the fading light and scattered it into verification patterns that looked, for a moment, like the northern lights had decided to attend a security conference.

"You see it now," Polly said from his shoulder. "What the Spire doesn't show you."

"The system is bigger than the building."

"The system is bigger than anyone who's ever tried to contain it. Izack knew that. It's why he built Avalon as a garden, not a fortress." Her feathers shifted. "Alexander chose fortress. That's not wrong. It's just not the whole answer."

"None of them are."

"Now you're getting somewhere," Polly said, and for once she didn't sound like she was grading him. She sounded like someone who had watched three hundred years of people learn the same lesson and was still, despite everything, moved by it.

Marcus opened his audit slate and began composing his first report. The words were technical. The implications were not.

*Perimeter defense network: operationally sound. Infrastructure integrity: compromised at architectural level. Forty percent of routing logic attributable to erased operator. Recommend: full system review, not replacement. The code works. The question is whether we can afford to keep pretending we don't know whose it is.*

He sealed the report with his Third Circle authority and sent it toward the Spire.

Then he turned east, toward Irondeep, where the Deepstone Engineers maintained the tongues like actual infrastructure and a probability-bending Thorne sister laughed at equations that floated around her like curious birds.

The audit was just beginning. The edges had more to teach him than the center ever could.



Chapter 20: The Industrial Hum

The transit circle deposited Marcus inside a mountain.

Not on a mountain, not near a mountain — inside it, three hundred meters below the surface, in a receiving hall carved from living granite with the kind of precision that made his engineering brain feel simultaneously impressed and personally attacked.

The hall was circular, fifty meters across, with walls polished to a mirror finish that reflected the arrival circle's light in repeating arcs. The ceiling arched overhead in a catenary curve — the exact shape a hanging chain makes under its own weight, inverted. Which meant someone had calculated the load-bearing geometry of this room before carving it, rather than approximating with a dome and hoping for the best.

Someone had done structural engineering with rock, on purpose, and gotten it right.

The tongues hit him before his eyes adjusted.

At the Spire, the Six Sacred Tongues sang. At the Floating Shelves, they whispered. Here in Irondeep, they *hummed* — a low, sustained, mechanical vibration that Marcus felt in his sternum before he heard it in the air. Not music. Not crystal resonance. The sound a well-maintained power grid makes when every transformer is operating at rated capacity and the load is balanced across all phases.

Marcus stood in the receiving hall and felt, for the first time since arriving in Aethermoor, like the system was speaking his language.

"Don't get comfortable," Polly said from his shoulder. "They've been speaking it longer."

* * *

A dwarf named Forvek Ironthread met them at the hall's exit.

Forvek was four feet tall, four feet wide at the shoulder, and carried a clipboard made from hammered copper that contained more organizational infrastructure than some departments Marcus had worked in on Earth. His beard was braided with wire that glowed faintly in alternating tongue-colors, which Marcus's detection faculty interpreted as a walking diagnostic array — each wire sampling a different frequency of the Protocol's output, the whole ensemble functioning as a real-time system health monitor worn as facial hair.

"Keeper Chen," Forvek said, checking the clipboard. "Third Circle authority. Field audit of tongue infrastructure. Estimated duration: three days. Assigned liaison: Thane Lyra Thorne."

"Thane?" Marcus asked.

"Honorary title. Given to non-dwarven researchers who contribute meaningfully to the guild's knowledge base. Lyra earned it by solving a load-balancing problem that had killed three Runic Engineers over four centuries." Forvek made a note on his clipboard. "She is in the probability chamber. Level twelve. I will take you there after orientation."

"Is orientation optional?"

Forvek looked at him the way a building inspector looks at someone who has just asked whether fire exits are really necessary.

"Orientation," Forvek said, "is not optional."

* * *

Orientation took two hours.

Forvek walked Marcus through Irondeep's infrastructure with the systematic thoroughness of someone who had been maintaining critical systems since before Marcus's civilization had invented writing, and who was politely certain that Marcus's civilization would not have needed to invent writing if it had built better systems in the first place.

The halls ran hundreds of miles in every direction, carved around a titanic stalactite that served as the city's central support column and primary ley-energy conductor. Rivers of molten ore flowed through channels cut into the walls, providing light and heat through a heat-exchange system that made the Spire's crystal infrastructure look like a college science project. Luminescent crystal veins ran along the ceiling in patterns that Marcus recognized, with a jolt, as circuit diagrams — not metaphorical circuit diagrams, not "looks like" circuit diagrams, actual circuit diagrams rendered in geological time by engineers who had been routing energy through crystal lattices since before the concept of electricity existed on Earth.

Nothing in Irondeep crossed blindly. Freight lifts, courier rails, slag channels, and maintenance crews all moved through pre-resolved corridors cut so precisely that conflict felt engineered out of the route before the route existed. The mountain did not improvise traffic. It adjudicated it. Marcus could feel why. Every lane carried a moving proof of right-of-way: not a static permission, but a locally signed agreement between load, timing, and witness. A freight cart could not simply replay yesterday's route through today's traffic. The mountain hashed the present state into the passage itself and invalidated stale assumptions with geological contempt.

Golem sentinels stood at intersections. Not Clay's kind of golem — not pact-born, not sentient, not persons. These were machines: stone automata running on runic programming, each one executing a patrol routine with the kind of deterministic precision that told Marcus they had been compiled, not composed. Someone had written their behavior in a formal specification language and then carved it into their operating matrix.

"Your detection faculty," Forvek said, watching Marcus study a sentinel's rune-work. "You are reading the code."

"Behavior specification. Patrol boundaries. Response thresholds. Exception handling." Marcus traced a line of runes that spiraled down the sentinel's left arm. "Is that... a watchdog timer?"

Forvek's expression shifted. On a human face, the change would have been subtle. On a dwarven face, which communicated primarily through the geometry of eyebrow movement, it was practically an ovation.

"You understand runic engineering."

"I understand engineering. The runes are the implementation language. The principles are the same."

"That," Forvek said, making another note on his clipboard, "is the first intelligent thing a surface visitor has said in my hall in forty years."

* * *

Level twelve was deeper than Marcus expected deep to go.

The elevator — a platform of carved stone that descended through a shaft lit by crystal veins, powered by ley-current counterweights that operated on principles Marcus recognized as equivalent to regenerative braking — dropped them through geological strata that told a story in compressed time. Granite. Basite. A layer of something that glowed with residual magical energy so old it predated the Protocol. Then harder stone, darker stone, stone that had been under pressure for so long it had forgotten how to be anything else.

The probability chamber was a spherical room carved at the intersection of three major ley-lines, positioned so that the natural energy currents created standing waves that rippled through the space in visible patterns. The walls were covered in equations — not written, not projected, but carved into the stone and then filled with luminescent crystal so they glowed with their own solutions, updating in real-time as the ley-currents shifted.

In the center of the room, surrounded by floating geometric figures that orbited her like a personal solar system of unsolved problems, Lyra Thorne was arguing with a chalkboard.

"No," she told it. "That's lazy and you know it."

The chalkboard, which was actually a slab of polished obsidian suspended at eye level by runic levitation, displayed an equation that rearranged itself in response to her objection, tried a different approach, and was immediately corrected again.

"Better. But the variance term is hiding a singularity. Expand it."

Quiris, her emerald companion, sat on top of the chalkboard like a small lizard-shaped tenure committee, watching the mathematics reorganize itself with the expression of something that had opinions about rigor.

Lyra saw Marcus before he announced himself. She was the kind of person who tracked entrances the way weather stations tracked pressure changes — not out of paranoia, but because new variables were interesting and she wanted to integrate them before they disrupted her current calculation.

"Keeper Chen." She smiled with the full wattage of someone who had been told she was too much her entire life and had decided that was everyone else's problem. "You survived Alexander. I'm genuinely impressed. Most people he doesn't like, he just waits out. There's a supply officer who's been trying to get a meeting with him since before the Second Incursion."

"He seemed... efficient."

"He's our brother. Efficiency is the nicest word for what he is." She waved a hand and the floating equations parted around Marcus like a school of mathematical fish making way for a larger predator. "Forvek give you the full orientation?"

"Two hours."

"That's the short version. He likes you." She dropped from the platform she'd been standing on — a disc of carved stone that hovered three feet off the ground, because apparently working at a normal height was too constraining for someone whose entire discipline was about refusing to be constrained. "So. Field audit. Tongue infrastructure integrity. You want to know if our system is holding."

"I want to know how your system works."

Lyra's expression shifted. The smile stayed, but something behind it sharpened — not defensively, but with the particular attention of someone who had been underestimated enough times to recognize when someone wasn't doing it.

"Why?"

"Because the Spire treats the tongues like sacred architecture. Alexander treats them like military infrastructure. And you — or the Deepstone Engineers, or both — treat them like..."

He paused, because the word he wanted felt too familiar to be appropriate. Like calling someone else's religion by your own name for it.

"Like systems," Lyra finished. "Go ahead. You can say it. You won't offend anyone down here. The Deepstone have been doing systems engineering for four thousand years. They just call it runic craft."

* * *

The audit was different here.

At the Floating Shelves, Marcus had found a system held together by one man's dedication and his dead brother's code. At Irondeep, he found a system that didn't need to be held together because it had been built correctly in the first place.

The tongue relays were integrated into the mountain's infrastructure the way plumbing is integrated into a building — not as a separate sacred system overlaid on mundane architecture, but as a fundamental utility. Every hall, every chamber, every corridor had tongue-verification built into its structural members. The crystal veins that lit the tunnels also carried Protocol data. The ley-current channels that powered the forges also maintained consensus fields. The golem sentinels that patrolled the intersections also served as mobile verification nodes.

Everything was dual-purpose. Everything was maintained. Everything was documented.

Forvek showed him the maintenance logs. They went back three thousand years. Three thousand years of scheduled inspections, calibration records, replacement schedules, and failure reports, each one filed in the same format, each one signed by the engineer who performed the work, each one cross-referenced with every other maintenance action that had occurred in the same ley-sector during the same period. The records were not merely archived. They were chained. Every repair entry carried the seal-mark of the last verified intervention in that sector, so falsifying one meant falsifying the structural memory of everything that followed.

Marcus stared at three millennia of infrastructure maintenance and felt something he had not felt since arriving in Aethermoor: professionally humbled.

"This is better than anything I've ever seen," he said.

Forvek's eyebrows performed a motion that communicated pride, acknowledgment, and the mild disappointment of someone who had hoped the surface world would have figured this out by now.

"The mountain does not care about politics," Forvek said. "If a crystal vein fails, it fails. If a ley-current shifts, it shifts. The mountain does not negotiate. We maintain, or we die. This has been true for four thousand years. It will be true for four thousand more."

"That's your engineering philosophy?"

"That is not philosophy. That is geology."

* * *

On the second evening, Lyra took Marcus to a chamber that the Deepstone called the Resonance Gallery — a natural cavern where the ley-lines converged in a pattern that created standing acoustic waves, making the stone itself sing in frequencies that corresponded to each of the six tongues.

They sat on a carved bench overlooking a river of molten ore that flowed two hundred feet below, its light painting the walls in shifting amber. The tongues hummed around them in their industrial chorus, and Marcus felt the Protocol operating with a density and stability he hadn't experienced anywhere else.

"This is what it's supposed to sound like," he said.

"This is what it sounds like when nobody's neglecting it." Lyra pulled a slate tile from her satchel and tossed it into the air, where it hung, rotating slowly, displaying probability distributions that updated in response to the ambient tongue-field. "The Spire has better crystal infrastructure. More sophisticated architecture. Izack built beautiful things. But he built like a poet. The Deepstone build like plumbers. And plumbing lasts longer than poetry."

"Your vault locks," Marcus said. "Forvek called them harmonic safety."

Lyra's grin flashed. "That's because 'cryptography with lungs' makes the elders grumpy. Deepstone vaults don't open to passphrases. They open to the right interval struck by the right people in the right witness order. A bad key can be stolen. A wrong chord collapses before it reaches the hinge. Even the right chord dies on replay if the chamber's load, temperature, and nearby signatures don't match the opening it was composed for. Harmonic locks don't trust memory. They trust live resonance."

"You're being harsh on your father."

"I'm being accurate about my father. He was a genius and a dreamer and he built a world because he didn't like being cold in a cave. I love him. I also maintain the systems he is literally living inside of, and I can tell you from professional experience that genius-designed systems are harder to maintain than boring ones. Especially when the genius is still in there having opinions about your calibration." She caught the slate tile without looking. "That's not a criticism. It's a maintenance report."

Marcus smiled despite himself. Lyra was easier to be around than Alexander — not because she was kinder, but because she processed tension through motion and humor instead of stillness and precision. Talking to Alexander felt like filing a report. Talking to Lyra felt like running to keep up with someone who was already three topics ahead and didn't intend to slow down.

He liked her instantly, which was safe in a way his feelings about Senna were not. Lyra invited momentum. Senna, alarmingly, made him want to stop moving long enough to be known.

"Your probability research," he said. "What are you actually doing down here?"

"Variance mapping." She gestured, and the equations that perpetually orbited her reformed into a three-dimensional model of Irondeep's ley-network, each node pulsing with probability distributions that showed the likelihood of failure at every point in the system. "The tongues aren't deterministic. They're stochastic. Every relay has a failure probability. Every ley-current has a drift rate. Every verification cycle has an error margin. The Spire pretends these margins don't exist because the system is sacred. Irondeep measures them because the system is real."

Marcus studied the model. It was beautiful — not aesthetically, but architecturally. Every failure mode mapped. Every cascade path identified. Every redundancy calculated and maintained.

"Did Kael's code reach here?"

The question landed like a stone in a still pond. Lyra's expression didn't change — her public mask was too fast for that — but Quiris shifted on her shoulder, and the floating equations hesitated in their orbits for exactly one point-three seconds before resuming.

"Yes," Lyra said. "Three routing optimizations. An anomaly filter. A tongue-weight rebalancing algorithm."

"And?"

"And the Deepstone Engineers found all four within six months, audited them against their own specifications, identified the three that were improvements and the one that was a security vulnerability, kept the three, removed the one, and filed the whole thing in their maintenance logs under 'Unauthorized Modifications: Resolved.'"

Marcus let that settle. "They caught it."

"They caught it because they audit everything. Because they maintain like the mountain doesn't negotiate. Because they have three thousand years of institutional discipline that doesn't care whether the person who wrote the code was a genius or a ghost or the founder's son." Lyra's voice stayed light, but the precision underneath was load-bearing. "They did what the Spire couldn't do. They looked at Kael's work objectively and made a technical decision instead of a political one."

"Alexander couldn't do that."

"Alexander couldn't afford to do that. He's alone on those shelves with twenty-three operators and nine hundred square kilometers of border. The Deepstone have four thousand engineers and an institutional memory that predates the Protocol. Different constraints produce different decisions." She rotated the probability model with one finger. "That's not a moral judgment. That's statistics."

* * *

Later, walking through the deeper halls where the crystal veins dimmed to a phosphorescent murmur and the tongue-hum dropped to a frequency Marcus felt more in his teeth than his ears, Lyra said something he didn't expect.

"You're going to visit Mira."

It wasn't a question.

"The Verdant Tithe is on the audit route," Marcus said carefully.

"The Verdant Tithe is on the audit route," Lyra repeated, with the tone of someone translating a bureaucratic sentence into its emotional content. "Sure. It's also where my twin sister has been living alone with dryads for thirty-one years because looking at any of us reminds her of the brother we all agreed to forget."

The silence after that was specific. Not uncomfortable — specific. The silence of someone who had chosen to say a true thing and was giving Marcus time to hear it.

"You and Mira are twins."

"Were twins. Are twins. Depends on whether you count the last three decades of not speaking." Lyra's hands were still. Marcus hadn't seen her hands be still before. "I'm the variance. She's the memory. We used to be a complete system. Now we're two halves running independently and neither of us wants to admit the sampling error."

Quiris pressed closer to Lyra's neck. A small gesture. The kind of thing a companion does when the person they're bonded to needs warmth and doesn't know how to ask.

"People think I got away clean," Lyra said. "The fun one. The one who left the Spire and went to do research and didn't carry anything heavy. Lyra's fine. Lyra's in Irondeep doing math and laughing at equations and being too fast for grief to land on."

"Did you?"

"Get away clean?" She looked at him with her father's dark eyes and something in them that wasn't speed or wit or shamelessness but the quiet exhaustion of someone who had been moving for thirty-one years because if she stopped the weight might arrive. "Nobody gets away clean. Some of us just run faster than the invoice."

The molten ore river pulsed below them. The tongue-hum vibrated in the stone.

"Mira understood Kael," Lyra said. "Better than anyone. Better than Alexander, who worked beside him. Better than Senna, who managed him. Mira understood Kael because they were the same kind of mind — interior, deep, pattern-seeking. The difference was that Mira trusted the system and Kael trusted himself." She paused. "When the erasure happened, Mira didn't argue. She didn't fight. She just... stopped. Stopped talking to us. Stopped coming to the Spire. Walked into the forest and let the dryads close the canopy behind her."

"Because?"

"Because we asked her to forget someone she understood, and she couldn't do it without forgetting the part of herself that understood him." Lyra's voice was quiet now, stripped of the speed that usually protected it. "The system asked her to choose between her integrity and her family. She chose integrity. And I chose motion. And Alexander chose duty. And Senna chose continuity. Four siblings, four coping strategies, none of them adequate."

"And Kael?"

"Kael chose to be right." The word sat between them like a closed fist. "He was right about the flaws. He was right about the inefficiency. He was right about the consensus delay. He was right about everything except what being right cost everyone who loved him."

Polly, who had been silent for longer than Marcus had ever known her to be silent, made a sound that was not a word and was not a breath and was, Marcus realized, the noise a three-hundred-year-old witness makes when someone finally says the thing she has been waiting to hear said aloud.

* * *

Marcus stayed at Irondeep for three days.

He audited every relay. Documented every maintenance log. Mapped every point where the Deepstone's infrastructure interfaced with the broader Protocol. And what he found was the clearest, most uncomfortable lesson of the audit so far:
